RATING: 9
This is currently my wife and my favorite two player game. We play with the 3-player setup, and it works fantastic. We get the satisfaction of "building" a castle, and most of our games have come down to just a few point swings. Admittedly, the game can suffer from analysis paralysis, and in 3 and 4 player games, there can be a lot of downtime between Master Builder turns. But, each turn, you are watching others build their castle, buy rooms that you want, and agonizing over under/overpricing something. The auction mechanic is neat, and the room variety is great. Add in the randomization of each set-up, and not knowing if you'll get what you want, and you have a very engaging board game experience. The only reason it's not a 10 in my book is the scoring track. Snake tracks aren't that great - but I don't have a solution otherwise. Great game - I'm always up for playing this.

RATING: 5
I enjoy this more than Suburbia for the more relaxed feel,  more attractive theme, and the price setting auction which requires me to keep some attention paid to what others are doing (this is really where CoMKL adds interaction between players compared to Suburbia). The initial setup really blows though, makes me want to play 2 or 3 games in a row as a result, and like Caverna, I highly recommend either making an insert or buying a custom one. Second, the same number of turns regardless of player count looks nice on paper, but causes the game to stumble as you remove the tiles at random (In some ways, I'm curious how it works if you do a 2p game with 3p tile counts, sure, it takes longer, but how does that fit). Still more attractive in terms of desire to play than Suburbia for us.

I really wish there was more value paid to the a constrained castle vs just this perpetual sprawl that it almost always morphs into. Getting pieces that match castle goals almost always trumps the adjacency benefits of doing urban-style density.

There is nothing WRONG with the game mechanics - it all works well and there are lots of strategic decisions to be made - but there isn't much of a sense of fun in the game, which is crazy considering the fascinating theme. I preferred it to Suburbia, but if I was to play a tile-laying game along these lines, I would still opt for Glen More every time.

RATING: 8
I have to say that the implementation is nowhere near elegant for a tile laying game. By saying this I am referring to the rule how you must align the tiny little doors on the tiles of varying shape and size. It would not be an issue, if all the tiles had a similar contact surface. However, this is not the case since you have to match, e.g., a circle with a rectangle. Having your tiles just 0.1 mm off can cause a situation where the next tile does not physically fit into to your preferred slot, or vice versa you can pull off illegal moves by shifting the other tiles slightly and squeezing an elephant into a keyhole.

Anyway, the above is maybe the only complaint I have about the game. I enjoyed the theme (which is quite strongly present for an eurogame), the market speculation, and of course the whole aspect of laying tiles and creating combos. Moreover, the market with a limited amount of money in circulation is a nice touch. Variable bonuses and tiles guarantee longevity, I reckon. Art is not great but not bad either. The individually named rooms add flavour to the game. You can almost feel how you are building a castle for a truly mad king.  

A small concern for some groups might be the expected brain burn when the starting player sets up the market at the beginning of the round. If you are playing with very tight players who necessarily want to gather and analyse all available information, this phase of the game can become a quite long and frustrating experience. For our group this was not a problem, though.

Castles of Mad King Ludwig has potential to become a classic.

RATING: N/A
This is a great, fun, and well designed game, although it is a bit slower and more fiddly than Suburbia... however let me tell you a story:

I bought the game at a con, as an experienced board gamer. The components were as I expected, as was the size of the box. However I can't help but reflect on how careless the product design is. A person new to the hobby, picks up this exciting looking game and buys it. They punch the game, and have a box full of tiny bits floating around. No insert, no baggies. They don't realise the game expects them to supply these and as a result the game sits unloved on a shelf since it is so fiddly to set up, and half the tiny pieces are missing since they fell out of the box.

Fundamentally, this reinforced to me, the idea that Ted Alspach (and Bezier Games) do not care about the ownership experience. they care about profit. The slight increase in a profit margin is much more important than the players. Here is a game built with love but presented with none. And that made me sad. It feels like we're not a community any more.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Ted Alspach's next big game from Bezier game's after his Mensa select hit, "Suburbia". At first blush it can be easy to look at the game's appearance and note the designer and think that it is similar to and should be compared to Suburbia but that would be a mistake. While there are whispers of commonality in some of the mechanics in this game it is a very different animal all together and deserves to be seen as such. Castle's theme is pretty solid and the narrative drive aligns well with play. You are playing the role of a royal ass hat who bankrupted his country building more and more elaborate and sometimes ridiculous castles. I read somewhere that the Disney castle is based on one of his. The reason this aligns with play is that you spend the game drafting rooms and using them to build your own personal castle. Each type of room has a power that triggers when placed next to other rooms in a similar way to the tile placement mechanic in Suburbia. There are of course secret and public objectives which you are building your castle to achieve in order to out score your opponent which adds a little variability to the game. The result is a bunch of crazy looking castles that leave you wanting to kill somebody. If the game has any controversy it would be found in the drafting mechanic which is the primary means of generating revenue. Each turn someone plays the role of master builder. Their job is to arrange the available flop of rooms in order along a draft line with prices in ascending order. The idea is, the builder gets revenue based on what people ultimately pay for drafting a room. So if the builder plays their arrangement well, they effectively balance their need for revenue with their opponents need for rooms while still not giving away what they need to win or what their opponent needs to achieve known game objectives. The problem is, valuation is pretty tricky and if you get it wrong either by missing certain details on the board or not understanding certain player biases beyond your goal than you are broke for an entire round. This... Pretty much costs you the game if you aren't careful. Still, another excellent game by Alspach and proof of his range as a designer. I am glad I own this game and will be keeping it in my collection. It will be interesting to see what he makes next. Hopefully, he'll show some range on components next time as well.

RATING: 7
I wonder what would happen if people started playing "Castles of Mad King Ludwig: the couples edition". You know, exactly the same game, but this time each castle is built by a pair of two human beings that, preferably, share the same roof in their non-boardgaming life. HE could finally impress HER with his Tetris-skills and create the optimal room plan, SHE could design the perfect harmony between living room curtains and grotto lighting. BOTH could discuss whether it might be a good idea to have the theater room directly adjacent to the kitchen. After the game is over, they could go back to their real home and, even though it will be much less impressive than the fantasy castle they just created, they will still happily agree that their fictitious castle was obviously MUCH better than that hell of a garden massacre that Liz and Pete were building. 
I really wonder when relationship therapists will realize the possibilities of this game ...

RATING: N/A
I really like this game. It takes the skeleton of Suburbia and does something pretty different with it. The various completion bonuses of the different room types and hooking all the little rooms together for synergies is pretty interesting. 

The "master builder" role of completely re-arranging the real estate market every round is bit of a love-hate mechanic. It's simultaneously fascinating and potentially frustrating. For experienced players, it's a strategic, engrossing decision. But newer players are almost guaranteed to break it. It requires a fair degree of familiarity with what kind of buildings are in the game in order to make good decisions about what things should be worth. 

This isn't specifically a bad thing. Experience should reward players. But (a) it means that new players throw balance a little bit out the window and (b) it can be an AP-inducing mechanic. Nevertheless, Mad King is fun, quirky, and neat.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
A game that strips out all the things I disliked about Suburbia, while retaining some similarities, both superficial and mechanical.  Ludwig's market, with the start player setting the prices of the available rooms, is perhaps its nicest feature.  The rest is about placing rooms with harmonious neighbours and chasing bonuses.  It all works perfectly well and I'd be reasonably happy to play again if it were suggested.

Given the beauty of the real Ludwig's castles, the game's artwork has to rate a missed opportunity.  There is a lot of drab grey and much of the art has a cheap, computer generated look.  Production quality is decent.

RATING: 7
Εδώ οι παίχτες χτίζουν ο καθένας το κάστρο του προσέχοντας πως θα τοποθετήσουν τα δωμάτια και τους διαδρόμους ώστε να πάρουν όσο το δυνατό περισσότερους πόντους. Αυτό που ξεχωρίζει στο παιχνίδι είναι ο μηχανισμός του Master Builder. Κάθε γύρα αυτός καθορίζει τις τιμές των δωματίων. Μετά οι υπόλοιποι παίκτες επιλέγουν πρώτοι τα δωμάτια που θέλουν πληρώνοντας τον Master Builder, ο οποίος επιλέγει τελευταίος και πληρώνει στη τράπεζα.

Το όλο πράμα δουλεύει πολύ καλά. Ο μηχανισμός είναι απλός και λειτουργικός. Πρέπει να δεις ποια δωμάτια ενδιαφέρουν τους υπόλοιπους και να θέσεις τις τιμές ανάλογα. Κατά τα άλλα υπάρχουν κάποια goals που είναι ανοιχτά από την αρχή του παιχνιδιού και δίνουν πόντους στο τέλος του παιχνιδιού. Πχ να έχεις τα πιο πολλά υπνοδωμάτια στο κάστρο σου.

Εκτός από τα φανερά goals, οι παίκτες έχουν και κάρτες (αποστολές) που επίσης δίνουν πόντους στο τέλος. Όλα αυτά δίνουν μια κατεύθυνση στους παίκτες προς τα πού να κινηθούν, ποια δωμάτια να κυνηγήσουν.

Στα αρνητικά να βάλω πως το παιχνίδι είναι κάπως overpriced αν το κρίνεις με βάση τα components τα οποία είναι απλά tiles και tokens με λιτά γραφικά. Επίσης όλα τα δωμάτια απλά δίνουν πόντους με βάση τα γειτονικά δωμάτια. Δεν υπάρχουν κάποια σπέσιαλ abilities, κάτι που θα έδινε κατά την γνώμη μου περισσότερο βάθος στο παιχνίδι. Από την άλλη βέβαια το ότι τα δωμάτια είναι απλά κάνει το παιχνίδι πιο προσβάσιμο στο ευρύ κοινό και έχει μόνο λίγα απλά εικονίδια πάνω στα tiles αντί για γραμμές κειμένου.

Αξιόλογο tile placement eurogame με απλούς κανόνες και γρήγορο ρυθμό. Ο μηχανισμός του master builder που θέτει τις τιμές είναι πολύ λειτουργικός και κάνει το παιχνίδι να ξεχωρίζει.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A castle building, "variable size tile" tile-laying game that creates a warm enjoyable gaming experience.  The pricing mechanic, somewhat to my surprise, worked nicely to create fun decisions, and the public and secret goals worked nicely in the game.  

Thinking about the design here, I can see how certain design issues were considered and capably addressed.  I found it fascinating how when this game was first explained to me, I firmly believed that the "door to door line up" issue was going to be a major design problem, particularly with all these disparate shapes, but after a few turns, the challenge of lining up doors felt interesting, and by the end of the game, this challenge managed to feel fresh, interesting, and a curious incentive to play the game again. 

The builder in us evidently enjoys lining up doors and building interesting looking castles.  As such, there is some magic in this game design.  Well done.  8 of 10 , November 2014

Going to raise Castles to a 9 for its sheer design brilliance.  Excellent re-playability, spatially, strategically and tactically.  Excellent expansion with the moats and swans.  October 2016.

RATING: 7
Very light and pretty fun design. And I really like games that (sort of) envolve architectural design.

The decisions of which room to buy and add to your middle age piece of real estate based on the numerous ways to score is the real heart of the game and it is pretty interesting. And it is by no means obvious.

However, building your castle and deciding in what position to fit the new additions is not as hard and possibly constrictive as it is in Factory Fun, for example, a game that I enjoy immensely. You will never feel the need to build your castle in a more "efficient" matter next time.

In Factory Fun, at the end of a match you sometimes feel really proud of your plant design, both in terms of aesthetics and efficiency. In this game, that is not much of a factor, which I found a little frustrating.

RATING: 9
No disrespect to Suburbia, but Castles is a VASTLY superior game (I rated Suburbia a 6).  

The tile laying and building selection (master builder) action is infinitely more interesting than Suburbia.  Also, they made the end game scoring for the shared goals friendly, so it isn't just a 1-1 race to get X goal.

The hidden goals from the utility rooms offered a nice strategic direction and the castles that we saw were extremely varied in size/shape/function.

RATING: 9
A lot of things to love about this game. Love the metagame of playing tetris and trying to fit the rooms together perfectly. The concept of having to pay the other players for room tiles is clever, creating really interesting decisions when pricing. If the other players get the rooms they want, they'll at least have to pay you a good amount for it which also benefits you. The theme is really fun as well. Really like the stories that can be created regarding who would actually live in your castle and how it would be utilized.

One downside I'm noticing is that games can seem a little repetitive. The decisions you make each turn can feel kind of the same, and there's little progression as the game goes on.

RATING: N/A
Finally get to play my first game of Castles of Mad King Ludwig with my gf yesterday at my FLGS.  I was so excited not only because it's my first time going to a gamer cafe, but because I finally get to play Mad King.  First impression, not as fun as I expected it to be due to the slower speed.  I'm prone to AP to begin with, so even with 2 players sometime I can take a bit of time to decide how to price the buildings.  I do like the placing and building of my own castles, love it even more when I was able to carry out my plan and chain some combos together.  The final scoring was fun and exciting as we reveal our secret objectives even though we missed handing out points for buildings whose stack has been depleted.  The game would benefit greatly from some good inserts, components are everywhere.  Conclusion, still like it even though not as much as I expected, would love to give it another try.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A lapkamenedzselős-lepakolgatós műfajban az egyik legjobb, amivel valaha játszottam. Viszonylag egyszerű szabály, de végtelen kombólehetőségek. Nincs két egyforma helyiség... Random kerülnek játékba a helyiségek, 10 féle méretben, és összesen 6 típusban. Minden méretben 3 féle típus fordul elő. Némi tematika is átjön, pl. a hálószoba mellett bünteti a játék ha zenetermet rakunk le. Jó dolog a 2 szint is, a különböző alakok is jól mutatnak(kis kör, nagy kör... pl.), és a 7 féle jutalom dobja fel legjobban, az ad neki igazán mélységet. Ami hátrány, az a nagyon nagy AP veszély. Nekünk a 2 fős parti volt tisztán bő 3 óra. A helyiségek sorbarendezése nagyon időigényes lehet, 4 főnél el se tudom képzelni meddig lehetne vele szüttyögni, ha tényleg mérlegeljük, hogy kinek mi a jó. Már ketten se nagyon volt rá esély hogy komolyan figyeljünk az ellenfél épületeire. Ennek ellenére pont ez a sorbarendezés adja a játék sava-borsát. Amire még figyelni kell, hogy elég nagy épületek jöhetnek ki, nekünk a játék 2/3-ánál arrébb kellett tolni az egyik teljes kastélyt, mert összeért a kettő, és nagyon macerás volt. A helyiség választásnál egyébként elég sok mindenre kell figyelni: van néhány építési szabály is, de ami nehezebb, hogy bizonyos bónuszok nem a szomszéd helyiségek után járnak csak, hanem a teljes kastélyunkra vonatkoznak, ezeket észben kell tartani, mert ha kimarad egy pontszám, azt utólag nehéz már követni. (Több száz pont szerezhető egy partiban.) Emellett nagyon fontos tényező a játékban, hogy az ellenfelet mennyire pénzeljük. Ha el tudjuk érni, hogy pénzszűkébe kerüljön (mert pl. a saját körünkben vesszük a drágább épületeket), akkor olcsóbban juthatunk épülethez, igaz hogy ilyenkor kevesebbet is fog tejelni az ellenfél... A sok pont ellenére nekünk nagyon kiegyenlített lett a 2 személyes partink, nüanszok döntöttek. Szerintem jól össze van rakva az egész, pont olyan bonyolult, amennyit még hajlandó vagyok újrajátszani ebből a típusból. Sokkal hamarabb ülnék le játszani vele, mint a Suburbiával, mondom ezt úgy, hogy mindkettővel 1 partin vagyok túl.
Ja mellesleg jól néz ki az egész: a szobák is, meg a lapkatároló táblák is...
---
Kellemes meglepetés: egy háromfős parti kevesebb, mint 2 óra alatt lement. De hogyhogy a kétszemélyes olyan hosszú volt a múltkor?

RATING: 8
Secrets (2015)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This one was a pleasant surprise. I can't compare it to Suburbia, but I liked it much better than The Capitals, another similar game.

The market mechanism is pretty neat. The last player in each round chooses the pricing for said round. But the money paid for each building in that round goes to that player. So, you have to carefully set prices such that the other players are willing to pay you large amounts for their needed buildings, while you get the one you want for cheap. It gives the game an interesting decision point (How high do I price the rooms other people want? How high do I price the room I want? What is my contingency plan in case someone buys the building I want?).

The room placement and comboing are also fun. You can create chains of closed rooms for additional actions, points, or other resources. The buildings have some odd shapes, which creates some cool logistical problems that are less Tetris-y and more Frank Lloyd Wright.

I think I'll probably end up getting this one, as it seems to have subtly deeper gameplay than its exterior would suggest.

RATING: 7.5
CoMKL is a very good follow-up to Suburbia, although I prefer the latter. After a half-dozen plays it's pretty apparent the game is a bit AP prone. Both games suffer from lengthy setup, in my opinion, especially with minimal player counts. But where Suburbia's Real Estate Market has a streamlined replacement system, CoMKL bogs down as the Master Builder creates the next iteration of the Contract Board. Although this does add a strategic element that Suburbia lacks, I don't believe a better game is created. However, I do enjoy it, and castle construction is just so darn cool. Though it takes awhile, I've never been disappointed with my creations.

RATING: 6
I played this for the first time at BGG.con 2014 and I didn't think I would like it because I don't like Suburbia.  While there are a lot of similarities, I like several things about Castles of Ludwig quite a bit better.  One is the way in which the market is set.  It is critical that the Master Builder decides quickly and doesn't fret about the details of where everything goes.  Also, the spatial aspect of the game is a huge plus, especially because my wife thrives on spatial play in games.  After two plays, I've rated this game a 7 but it is very close to an 8 for me.  Part of that is definitely because I see this having the potential to be a game that my wife really enjoys.  It's fine with 2 players, but gets more interesting with more.

RATING: 7.5
Nagyszerű hangulatot adó játék. Nekem a grafika részletes kidolgozottsága nagyon tetszik, bár sokan kritizálják. Az elemek karton vastagsága viszont szégyenletes. Bőven lett volna benne hely normális vastagságú kartonokra, de gondolom megint a pénz, bár az ára elég borsos. A külső doboz véletlenül sem illeszkedik a polcon a többi játék dobozához. Ezt sem értem miért, mert nincs benne olyan, melyet nem lehetett volna egy kicsit kisebbre szabni. A kiadótól van másik játékom, de annak a doboza szabvány méretű.
A játék viszont nagyon tetszik. Legfőbbként az tetszik, ahogy magunk rendezzük be, mely kastélyelem mennyibe kerül úgy, hogy minél több bevételhez jussunk a többi játékostól miközben a számunka kiszemelt elem megmaradjon a kör végére. Érdekes kastélyok alakulnak ki a játék végére és számtalan kombinációt össze lehet állítani. A játékban elég sok szempontot lehet figyelembe venni, hogy elérjük a a győzelmet. Ennek előnye az, hogy képtelenség minden lehetőséget kiszámolni. Folyamatosan változnak a lehetőségek. Én kifejezetten örülök, hogy megvettem :)

RATING: 8
Having been to Linderhof and Neuschwanstein on my trip to Germany, this caught my eye immediately.  The rules are very simple.  It's a cool medium light tile laying game and it's fun to watch your castle take shape.  The different rooms are cool, Secret Lair etc.  The main aspects are placing your rooms to maximize your points and also the master builder mechanic, which is basically one player setting the prices of tiles then he gets the money spent on tiles that turn but also goes last.  It's really a great mechanic- it's simple and easy to understand but it creates some excruciating decisions and creates a lot of indirect interaction.  You need to be looking at other players castles and you want them to pay you as much as possible for the rooms they are likely to buy, of course if you price them too high they will take something else or pass and take some free cash, and you also want to place the room you want so you can get it cheap enough but so other players won't buy it.  Very cool.  I like this better than Suburbia in virtually every way- it's quicker, it's more fun and prettier to build the castle than the city, it's less fiddly with cube pushing and arithmetic, and more interactive.

:thumbsup: [b]POSITIVE POINTS:[/b]

++ Good tactical tile-laying game with an auction mechanism to buy the tiles to build our own castle.
++ I like that you have to manage the spacial element of the tiles in your castle to respect some criteria (proximity from other type of room to combo with them) which for the most part feel thematic.
++ I like the funky-shaped tiles (and the humour in some of the room names) and the the match with the theme (the Mad King Ludwig wants a funky looking castle).


:thumbsdown: [b]NEGATIVE POINTS:[/b]

-- (Physical version only) The setup and tear down is too much for me, because of all the different shapes we need to sort out, also with the fact that the game doesn't come with an insert to help on this matter.
-- (Physical version only) I find this game too fiddly and with a lot of upkeep, as we always have to calculate every points we do from the room itself, the adjacent rooms' bonus and the downstairs room bonus. The final scoring is also fiddly. 
-- Even though there is a potentially great mechanism with the Master Builder, it's hard to efficiently play since there is a lot of small icons to check in every player's tableau to gauge the value on each tiles. So this feel in the end too fiddly and the game is either too multiplayer solitaire (like at the full player count if people doesn't care to carefully evaluate the tiles and price them randomly - so in the end you make do with what you have ans thus the mechanism isn't really new), or it leads to endless AP if the players want to correctly value the tiles (according to the tile points, the tile adjacency bonuses, the in-game bonus, etc.).
-- Mostly tactical with just a bit of strategy (not much long-term planning can be done), so in the end, I think Suburbia is the better game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A very nice game of tile placement, where you want to obtain and place tiles in such a way that they combo off one another to score you the most points possible.

Some nice extras were added to complicate that decision making, such as public goals (making certain tiles more valuable), private goals which differentiate what each player is most interested in, and bonuses that trigger when you complete rooms of your castle.

Also, makes good use of a pseudo "I split, you choose" mechanism, where the start player gets to set the prices of all the rooms, but is the last player to buy.  All payments are made to the start player, so he wants to entice people into paying for the high priced tiles, while getting the tile he wanted for as cheap as possible.

Overall, just a good solid game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Castles of Mad King Ludwig is the latest effort from designer Ted Alspach and publisher Bezier Games. Here, players compete to build the single castle most admired by the titular mad king, purchasing and then arranging variously shaped rooms and corridors to create a synergetic whole.

Alspach is perhaps best known to board game geeks for his 2012 hit Suburbia (though I am personally fond of last year’s One Night Ultimate Werewolf). As a design, Castles appears to be very much in the same vein as Suburbia, and I suspect both will appeal to a similar audience. However, the gamers I played with uniformly considered Castles to be the better design, and I must admit that I fell in love with this game despite my actively avoiding Suburbia just a few years earlier.

For a prototype, the gameplay of Castles was amazingly polished, and it would not surprise me if very little changes between now and its publication (which I believe is scheduled for Essen). Each round begins with an auction-like mechanic, in which the round’s “master builder” sets the prices of the available rooms. Players then purchase these rooms from the master builder (paying him/her directly), meaning that careful attention to the needs and capabilities of other players is crucial to good play during the price-setting phase. Players then add those rooms to their existing castles, attempting (and usually failing) to configure the Tetris-like shapes in a logical and beneficial manner. The rooms each provide individual benefits, as well as synergy bonuses or penalties based on adjacency to other room types. Although all players share a number of common goals each game, players can also accumulate individual goal cards, so players’ incentives tend to quickly and dramatically diverge. It was a personal treat to watch each castle unfold, and Ted has invested considerable work into making each tile unique and frequently playful.

The playing time ran just about 90 minutes with four players, and the combination of some healthy (read: non-destructive) interaction with Euro-friendly individual player-board optimization leads me to expect big things for the finished game. Having not played Suburbia, the comparison that initially came to my mind was Princes of Florence (though Castles is almost certainly less cutthroat, even with experienced players), a classic design that strikes a very similar balance. All-in-all, Castles of Mad King Ludwig should be yet another instant purchase for me upon release, and, due to its accessibility and smooth play, will likely see more table time in 2015 than any of the other games I tried at the Gathering.

RATING: 6
While I prefer building my castle to building my city in Suburbia, I prefer the automatic ordering of the Suburbia market to the Castles market. The Castle looks cooler than the city, way cooler. That's a big plus. It doesn't have the cool factor of building a city mind, but it makes up for that with having far less must track but difficult to do so information. On the other hand, Suburbia's market is fast. It lets you get to the cool part of the game, building, quickly. Castle's market, if you were to take it seriously, would require you to look at everyone's money on hand, their open slots (in terms of space available as well as bonuses) and what cards you think they have for scoring. Which is boring. Very boring. And long. And solo play while everyone stares at you wishing you'd get on with it. If you don't take it seriously, it makes the game very random and the winner will likely be determine by a bad ordering more than any skillful decisions they made.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Love it. Simple and well-paced, with a ton of agonizing decisions and tension around the Master Builder mechanic (i.e., how can I maximize dicking over my friends while not having it backfire spectacularly?) Room completion bonuses also give the game the little extra strategical oomph it needs. Changing goals (both secret and open!) and tile randomization make for good replay value.

I think this is actually best as a two player game. With more players, it's fairly impossible to keep tabs on what everyone wants, so a lot of the tension in the Master Builder mechanic evaporates. With two players things feel much more cutthroat and calculated. With more, this drops down to like a 7 for me.

My only real complaint is... I sort of wish the game were longer? In minutes this game lasts about my ideal length, but for once I think it would have been a better emotional experience if it were just a little bit longer. As it is, the game tends to end and I feel like I've only just begun building the start of the eccentric sprawling castle of my dreams. It always ends up feeling incomplete.

This is one of the few tile laying games I've played where both tile acquisition AND placement are truly interesting, and as such it may just be my new go-to for the genre.

RATING: 3.5
It is truly pathetic how Ted Alspach/Bezier Games have sold out, and allowed a far superior version WITH mini-expansion to be released, solely within one country who refuse to ship to anywhere else. That they remain silent, and let someone else produce a far better version of their own game, is rather telling...

As it stands, mechanically a great building title, with an innovative level of player interaction (through the master architect role), and a fantastic amount of replayability (the randomly determined desired attribute and bonus scoring cards). Yet structures never feel 'impressive'... a sinificant shortcoming for a game of this nature.

The production values... that's a different story. Box is ever-so-slightly larger than standard, which is annoying, tile art is bland and pretty dire, lack of insert makes storage tricky, there is so much wasted space inside the box (which could have been half the depth it is comfortably), and the cards (also dull) are paper-thin. I expected much more for the relatively high RRP. The start player token is pretty nifty.

Mechanics rating: 7, production values: 2.

RATING: 10
This game is similar to Suburbia, which I enjoyed quite a bit.  CoMKL is not a derivative however, it has enough thematic and gameplay difference to feel uniquely different and worthy as it's own standalone game.

I liked the mechanic of the "master builder" rotating each round, where one player is essentially deciding the prices of the available rooms in the market and all other players have to pay him/her.  The game revolves around that central conflict, and allows for secret agendas to score surprise comeback victories towards the end.  

The secret agendas are not too arbitrary either, there are ways to get more of them throughout the game, as well as public agendas that everyone is striving for.  

This gives every player a strong sense of motivation to make goal driven decisions as the game progresses.  It also feels pretty fair, like no one person is going to run away with the game at any time. 

All in all, once the basic rules are learned it's a deep game with lots of replay value, which is exactly what makes it a 10/10 for me
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Fun game! 

Would be better priced at a lower point as it doesn't offer the same production value as other 60$ msrp games (and some 40$ msrpg games). 

The components would be fine if the game were cheaper, but at the 55$ I paid, I definitely cannot shake the feeling that they could've at least given me standard sized cards, thicker main boards that don't stop working after a while, and an actual place to put the components. 

New buyers literally will have to put two separate decks of cards in ziploc bags! They'll get damaged very quickly this way, but Bezier Games doesn't offer buyers ANY alternative out of the box as there's zero storage solution for this game. The game basically damages itself unless the buyer finds a way to keep the components safe inside (or outside) the box. No game publisher should want this - it shows they don't card about their game after it is purchased and in the hands of the consumer.

Very cheap and lazy for such a premium priced game. There's no reason this game should be in this price range. 

If it's going to cut corners, that's fine. The components work and ziploc bags + some 2$ card boxes solve the issues. But this is not a 20-30$ game. It's a premium priced game. Imagine if Galaxy Trucker came without an operating insert, or Lords of Waterdeep (which is cheaper than this)?

The components are workable, but the price should reflect the many cheap corners Bezier Games took with this game components-wise and storage-wise. Paying 60$ for any game and getting hobbit-sized cards just sinks your heart and makes you feel like you should've gotten a FFG or DoW game instead.

But the game is still a lot of fun. Just makes you a little sad that the developer doesn't seem to really care about offering proper component value for the price they are asking. Couldn't they just bump the price down if they didn't want to offer better components? 

It's fine that the cards are tiny and the cardboard is super thin. But when Bezier Games made these component decisions, the price of the game should have gone down as well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
Shares the weaknesses of "Suburbia" (most notably, the fact that everybody is basically building in isolation). Also shares the many positives - public/private goals, opportunities for clever chaining of synergistic tiles, etc. - while managing to add new pluses.

The best of these changes is to the market. No more simply moving every tile on the market one space to the right; now you have to worry about how the Master Builder is going to rearrange the tiles. If he places a tile in an expensive slot, you'll have to decide it it's worth it to pony up. And if you do pony up, that money goes not to the bank but the the Builder. In other words, you may be empowering your opponent by splurging on a desired tile. That's a clever consideration, and it adds a smidge of player interaction to a gaming system that badly needs it.

I also appreciate the look of the castles when the game ends. The suburbs of "Suburbia" tend to be boring hexagonal mosh pits. But the castles here are asymmetrical and more interesting to look at.

One downer brought on by the transition from the hexagons of "Suburbia" is that the slightest brush will send your castle spinning across the table. Also irritating? The scoreboard is flimsy as heck. Keep tabs on your scores because one bump and you'll have no idea what the scores are.

RATING: 8
Remember Ted Alspach's Suburbia being near the top of my 2012 list? Well, he topped himself here in its spiritual successor. It captures the same building spirit of Suburbia, but focuses it on designing a castle instead of a city. Theme aside, the most noteworthy change is the removal of reputation and income (a very, very fiddly mechanism from Suburbia) with the addition of a master builder who sets the price and gets paid for the individual pieces of the castle. It's a very simple mechanism that adds much more interesting gameplay elements.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Better than expected. Been a hit with most gamers, across three games. Nice mostly closed money system. Rather than just buying stuff from the bank, like in Suburbia, active player gets to set prices of stuff for everyone at the table for a round (prices don't slide after each item bought), and collect money spent by other players. So you want to price things such that players are willing to pay, but not give them too good of a deal, and finally, still have the thing available that you (the active player) want, priced high enough that it doesn't get bought out from under you by other players (active player chooses last), but isn't more expensive that you're willing to pay.

That's actually the most interesting part to me. Building the physical layout of the castle looks like the core, but feels like a tertiary concern.

It also has a nice balance of public and private goals.

Overall more fun than Suburbia due to slightly less chained reactions that need to be detected and checked. Suburbia remains a great iPad app, but I prefer Castles for putting on the table.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.4
I was unsure if I really needed this game since it was such a clear derivative of Suburbia, but my husband really wanted it, so it became his birthday present. I'm delighted to say it's a strong game in its own right, and perhaps there's a place for both games on our shelf.

The key differences include a combination of public and private goals in constructing your castle. There is also a set limit on the length of the game based on which tiles get chosen, which makes this generally shorter than its predecessor. Lastly, the market setup is decidedly different.

Beautiful to look at and fun to play, I look forward to spending more time watching this game reveal its mysteries.

RATING: 6
Very different feel from Suburbia despite some surface similarities. Other players' castles are only relevant for public-goal competition (less tableau interactivity) but the pricing is human-mediated and paid to another player, which changes everything - it's the centerpiece of the game, the dynamic around which everything else turns. On a pure visual / aesthetic level, the different shapes and colors of rooms give every player's castle a distinctive look. I've always loved mazes and maps, and being able to imagine the building I'm building is really neat.

I'm faintly dissatisfied with the Bonus Cards - they seem a little swingy (some work really well together, some are mutually exclusive) and not terribly thematic - and more than faintly dissatisfied at the "one building per price" constraint. Turns where many rooms seemed of similar utility, people just bought the 1/2/4, giving the MB $7 income and leaving only $6-and-up rooms. Sometimes this happened where all the rooms were equally mediocre, sometimes where they were equally awesome; in both cases, it made the "auction" less interesting and hurt the Master Builder for the following round of turns.

I find it hard to believe the designer didn't try "more than one room may be offered at a given price", so I imagine there must be some reason the game isn't structured that way. It seems like the Master Builder has strong incentive to price things at fair market value (too high and they don't get paid) - though leftover money isn't worth much in final scoring, so you might end up degenerating to "everyone buys only from themself" for the latter portion of the game.

A friend has suggested "the MB may pay $1 to shift a room 1 slot left or right, any number of times". Another possibility is to make leftover cash worth more. I'd like to try both, but haven't gotten it to the table in a while.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.3
Honestly? This game looks ugly and it looks like a bad game but it is really, really good. It really delivers the theme of building a crazy castle. It goes so far as to say "in my castle we would watch movies and then go get something to eat and then we might go down the hall through the pumpkin patch to pet the horses." See that was my town. In Cara's town at the same very point all there was was stairs leading to an underground bedroom, a mushroom growing room, and a place called the hole with shackles on the walls. What this does is add necessary fun banter by allowing players to tell a fantasy story about their stuff. In Innovation I ask myself sometimes what do my people know and who are they? I like the fantasy story, not just the game mechanics. I get a richer experience from overplaying the game because I value what more casual lighter weight gamers value, regardless if I get bored with the lack of mechanics or levers to play with in those light weight games. In Castles of Mad King Ludwig though it doesn't feel like creating the story is me forcing it onto the game as much as it does as when I make Innovation do that. 

All of that said I also value artwork and at least 70% of this game looks like prison walls. I look at a lot of it and wonder why a better, busier artist was not hired. I do not feel like looking at so much space of gray bricks. Maybe that is what made King Ludwig go crazy. You don't have to make them colored bricks but can they not have some weathered shading of brown or some moss growing? Also the room layouts do not look good in photos, but play well and deliver the theme. The title plus the look of the game makes it sound like Kickstarter trash to people that have not played it. After playing it, the title also delivers the theme. 

The absolute busy ap inducing gameplay here on top of all of its pluses? Whew whee this is Ted's masterwork NOT Suburbia. He got even better at making games. It does not play so very light with two players. It plays slow and hard. I will never play this game fast because the little decisions could matter if I am noticing things. Also it has a maybe discover their secret goals nosy Hobbit factor and it really works. It is a huge package for something that was 100% localized in America and inexpensive. It ranks 45 on bgg for very good reasons. Cara gives it a 10. I have to rank it to see what my rating would be, as I determine ratings based on its placement on a pit each against each but only when you are sure enough, rankings list.

RATING: 6.5
Provisional rating after one play.  The good: it really feels like you are building a castle, albeit with half-drunk architects.  The setup of the market with Master Builder is very good.  The bad:  lots of luck in the tile draw.  Like Suburbia, sometimes it will come to you to take a tile and there are no tiles that fit your engine.  Strong left-right binding.  A weak Master Builder can unfairly help the player to their left.  I would prefer to see a variable player order.  Maybe the turn order could be based on how much you spent the previous turn.  If you got a cheap tile, you go to the back of the line next turn.

After 2 plays:  Even if you get some great combos going, if the tiles you need don't come, tough luck.  I think this makes it a good family game, but players wanting a heavier game will be frustrated.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
My wife got this for me for Christmas and it's quickly become a family favorite. Lots of options and things to make the game different each time. My wife isn't a huge gamer, but she latched onto this and wanted to play it over and over. It's still in her top 3. 

There is strategy to what you're doing, but it's not overwhelming and you don't have to think too deeply to be competitive. Your strategy will come in what bonus cards you choose (extra points at the end of the game for having certain features in your castle) and what favor tiles are revealed for more endgame points. Obviously, if points are given out for Outdoor rooms, you may want to try for those. There is also some strategy in placing tiles to be purchased. If I see that I'm the only one with a staircase, I know I can put the downstairs room in the cheapest slot. This means everyone has to pay me a little more for the rooms they want and I can pay the cheapest price for the downstairs room I want. Is someone going after a certain room type? You can choose to set the price a bit higher and hope they'll spring the extra cash for it. You also have some strategy on getting more points based on placement in your castle. You CAN agonize over placement if you REALLY want, but generally, it won't make a huge difference.

Really like the game and I'm thinking of getting the expansion in the near future. If you're a fan of games like Carcassonne, Alhambra, or maybe Agricola, you will probably enjoy this game as well.

RATING: 8
Nice game with different scoring possibilities (personal scoring objectives, scoring objectives for all players, direct value of rooms and bonuspoints to be gained depending on room interconnection conditions). Completing a room (connecting all entrances to other rooms or corridors or stairs) gives you a bonus action which depends on the type of room you completed. Every game is different because the scoring objectives are variable. Money circulates among the players as they take turns in selling rooms.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Entertaining light tile placement/set collection game. Aim for a close, compact castle to maximise bonus points due to adjacency. Smaller rooms can be completed easier. Only spread out if bonus cards trigger from Activity or Downstairs rooms, minimising Hallways which cost an action but give few points. Don't forget that stairs trigger room completion bonus.(July 2015)

Completing Utility rooms early means extra Bonus Cards and the time to factor them into room selection for the rest of the game. Don't hesitate to over-purchase the round before you're Master Builder. (August 2015)

RATING: 6
Falls into the same pits as Suburbia, in that the tiles you plan to get early may not ever be available to you the rest of the game.  Consequently, you can spend the really long game constantly in plan B, C, and D mode.  Too much tactics and not enough strategy, for a game of this length.  And the length is exacerbated by the Master Builder phase, which necessarily requires the game to crash to a halt every round.  Still, I like building with the irregular shapes and sizes.  And I like the streamlined powers that lead to simple calculations.  I would rather play this than Suburbia.

RATING: 3
I don't know where to start with the issues on this game.

Values are set by guesstimating the willingness to pay of your opponents; and blunders here can beggar the price-setter or hand great tiles away for a pittance.

The bonuses granted for completion of certain rooms can vary substantially, from free turns to very little.

The ability to plan or even hem in the chaos is minimal at best. 

Far from being a "strategy game" this falls into the category of "social exercise with scoring at the end".

RATING: 6
I don't like this one quite as much as suburbia.  
Partly that's because it has the same problem as that game, only worse.  Namely, that it feels too short, as though most of the effects in the game aren't going to matter.  Suburbia has an awful lot of buildings that count the things around them and care which types they're placed next to.  But early ones have to be placed next to your board, which doesn't have a type, and later ones aren't going to be filled in around (especially if you use doublers aggressively), a problem which only gets worse if you place borders/lakes.  So, you'll be lucky if you get even 3 residential buildings around your gas station/movie theater/whatever.  But in Castles, [i]every[/i] room cares about its neighbors.  And reciprocal relationships seem rare.  Then you add in the difficulty of placement (which suburbia didn't have, since everything was a hexagon), and trying to get the doors to line up, and the frustration mounts.  I've yet to play a game of this where I didn't feel like I was just slapping rooms together without any real plan.  And even if you do have a plan, it's going to be tough-to-impossible to execute.  You want to close off rooms by adding neighboring rooms with matching doors, but putting together any type of lattice or circle is an uphill battle.  You have no control over what comes out, nor what the other players price things at or buy (more on that later).  So, this unstructured feel fits the theme of the game, but is pretty annoying when you're trying to maximize your points.

The other irritation is the room purchase.  Suburbia's standard queue had its problems.  It was too turn-order dependent, and in a game where money's so tight, too expensive to purchase things up the line.  Castles kills some of those problems, but introduces others.  Now, there's no fixed price for any room.  You can guess, based on the power level & bonuses on offer, what people are likely to buy.  But it's just a guess.  And there really isn't much difference between one room and another, a lot of the time.  Often, players will just snag the cheapest thing available.  Later on, you get info about rooms people have collected, but there always has to be a cheap option and a prohibitively expensive one.  And if things don't go the way you want, you can miss out on your main source of income.  Since there's no carryover from round to round beyond the incentivizing money, the next player in line can undo all your work to keep rooms out of the hands of players who want them.

There's also some swingy-ness in the room types, with many being difficult to close, but the utility ones closing with zero effort and handing out bonus cards worth potentially a lot.  The bonuses themselves vary wildly, with some giving decent points for things you're going to be doing anyway, and others that are hard to do and just not worth it.  The only other type that really stands out is the living rooms, which can score again on closing.  If you devote some effort to it, you can surround a valuable room and score serious points here.

Anyway, with all the chaos going around in the way the tiles come out & are bought, it feels like we're shuffling cards around for an hour, then someone wins.  How?  No idea.  It's currently also suffering from the 'cult-of-the-new'/overplayed syndrome, so it may go up a notch after that wears off.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Great game.  Different from Suburbia and offers a different sort of experience.  I think given a choice I would take Castles over Suburbia but it's very close.  

Suburbia offers an engine building experience to score points which can be extremely rewarding but also more nuanced and tense.  Most common thought is "What city tile can I feasibly place to improve or cash in on my income/reputation engine?"

Meanwhile Ludwig offers up fun factor with tile laying and differing room sizes, bonus actions according to room type, and a simpler scoring consideration.  Most common thought is "What room can I feasibly place to score the most future and present points?"

IMO get Suburbia if you enjoy the theme and like building an engine in your games.  Otherwise go with Castles for ease of scoring and fun!

RATING: 7.5
2014 Essen has so far been a bit of a disappointment in terms of new Euros, though I am still to play some of the hotness like Caban and Panamax.  But I have been pleasantly taken by The Castles of Mad King Ludwig.  It is a satisfying game to play, even when you play badly as I do.  The building of your castle is just fun, in a way that building with lego is fun.  Having a Mould Room at the bottom of a staircase leading off your Music Room, with an outside Boot Room, an internal Shed and a Toilet next to your Greenhouse...it just feels good creating such a thing.  

The gameplay is smooth and easy, though there is a little bit of Surburbia-style fiddley scoring at times, it is all manageable and the result is a far more satisfying game than Surburbia.   

What keeps it from greatness is that there is something a little less integrated about the way the payment system works.  The Master Builder for the round determines the price of each section of building  and gets paid for each piece by the other players.  It works, but it does slow the game down as the Master Builder can take an age identifying who needs which piece and then calculating how much they think they can get for each piece, how much each person REALLY wants each piece.  In one game we played one player got fed up with that part of the game and randomly put the pieces into the different value boxes, and interestingly he did as well doing that as others did trying to calculate it.  

The game is suited to lighter gamers too.  And I would possibly play a variant with gateway gamers where tiles are drawn at random into the price boxes.  As evidenced in the game we played, it did not make THAT much difference playing that way and it cuts down on almost all of the downtime.  Played this way I reckon the game will last 45 mins for four as you are each only laying around 11 pieces of castle.

RATING: 4
If have had rated this 2 years ago it might be a little higher. The main attraction here is you can be creative building your castle layout. The issue i have with it is the arbitrary scoring, as the game from a competitive standpoint is entirely built around maximizing score. You score points for; placing rooms, putting other rooms at all the exits to a room, getting bonuses, hidden objectives, and end game bonus tiles, and your cash. This ends up being needlessly convoluted for a simple tile placement game that feels as though score is just meaningless nonsense than anything 'thematic'. Also it results in players miscalculating their scores and recounting, which slows things down and adds to the tedium.

RATING: 7
I have never played Suburbia, and it seems that this game cannot be mentioned without mentioning Suburbia in hand.  So for my part, that's my mention.  On the plus side, you can take this comment as an unbiased opinion without the cloud of Suburbia hanging over its head.

First off, there is a lot of substantial 'nothing' inside the box.  I don't know why they decided to make such a big box for such a compact game, but whatever.

As for the game itself, it's quite fun and engaging.  It's entirely about the "master builder's" choices as optimal placement of rooms becomes obvious depending on how you've originally laid out your first few tiles.  Thus, it becomes a problem accomplishing what you want to do because certain rooms prove to be a rarity among others and chances are someone is going to nab what you want before you can get a chance to do so.  For this reason alone, I would have preferred a bidding mechanic over the master builder setting the prices for rooms, but it's not a dealbreaker by any means.  The end result is that even though you might have a clear plan and direction, you still need to maintain the ability to adapt, to sacrifice certain bonuses to facilitate others, and there's lots of great decisions to be made.

Good game.  I have no idea how it compares to Suburbia.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This is a game where players are building castles and trying to earn points through the following ways:

-Public goals
-Private goals
-Placing certain tiles next to other tiles
-The tiles themselves are worth VP
-Focusing on specific types of rooms (for the basement tile scoring)
-Completing rooms with tile placements for special benefits (including straight up scoring)

This also has an offshoot of the "I split, you choose" mechanism.  I say an offshoot because each round a Master Builder actually places tiles on a market of ascending/descending prices. The Master Builder buys last and all purchases by other players go to the Master Builder.  It's really quite clever but will have the potential for major analysis paralysis for people who have to calculate what each tile is potentially worth for each player. These kind of issues usually has more to do with the players than the game, so it's not a huge issue. 

The game graphics for the first North American edition are pretty stark/bland. I have seen pictures of a foreign language edition that looks much better graphically, but I'm not sure if it would hurt the playability of the game. Either way it was something worth noting. For people who like 'seeing what they've created' (ie farms in Caverna/Agricola), this will definitely have a high appeal to those players.

Overall, it's a good game. I haven't played Suburbia at the time of writing this, but I hear they are similar. I think this is the more popular of the two, and Suburbia can be played on iOS, so that's something to be considered as well.

RATING: 7
[1 play] I enjoyed this game, with some reservations.  It was most frequently compared to a game I loathed, Suburbia.  There are indeed similar elements of tile laying and variable public/private goals.  But it does not have the problematic “everything affects everything” aspect of Suburbia, and I found the theme here infinitely more appealing.  You’re building a castle, and since you’re selecting from tiles that emerge randomly from a larger set, your castle will inevitably be a sprawling, deranged piece of improvisational architecture.  

The game has good clear rules and was easy to learn.  Production is good too; the tiles are full of entertaining detail, but the features that matter to gameplay stand out easily. One thing I especially appreciated is that rooms of different size are different shapes.  Square footage of various parts of your castle matters for some of the bonuses, and because size is coded to shape, it’s easy to look around and see what everyone has.  So much better than the countless games where you have a strategic need to know what your opponent is doing on some personal card tableau that you have no hope of being physically able to see.  

The biggest negative, which we swiftly perceived, is that there’s one aspect of the game that has potential to really slow things down.  On each round, the player who’s the master builder gets to deal out new tiles to fill back up to seven, and then gets to arrange those seven tiles any way they like along a fixed pricing spectrum.  When you’re the master builder, you’re going to get paid by other players for the tiles they choose, and you’ll have the chance to buy one yourself last. So ideally you want to think about what the other players are all doing and what everyone needs and how valuable the tiles you have to choose from are likely to be to different people, and then set prices so that the stuff others need is expensive and you make a bunch of money, and so that the thing you want yourself is still available but at a good price.  You can see how this is a complex decision and could make things drag.  

Other games this was similar to: 
Vinci for the “stuff you pass over becomes worth more” mechanic, here expressed in that at the end of each round, the tiles not taken get some money added to them.  Even a tile that nobody is excited about will eventually accumulate money until someone wants it. 
Modern Art for the way the players all make money by selling their stuff to each other. The economy isn’t completely closed, though. The master builder pays the bank, and there’s one kind of room that generates cash bonuses from the bank.  
Vegas Showdown for the tile laying element; it’s not within a constricted space as in VS, but like VS it has rooms linked up with doorways, and even more than in VS the relationship of what’s connected to what matters.  
Vegas Showdown even more for the tile selection:  to refill tiles, you deal cards from a deck and each card indicates a size/shape of tile.  Unlike VS the cards don’t have a second function, but their tile-picking function is just like VS. 

Would I play this again?  Certainly, and I’m likely to.  I don’t know whether it will rise or fall in my esteem when I play it some more, but I would be at least initially happy to do so.  There was exactly one copy of the game at our con, and it was totally the hotness; it barely got back in the box some days because as soon as one game finished, there’d be another table of people queued up to play it.  

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Like Suburbia, you're laying tiles down for combinatorial benefits, and I found reasonable fun here watching my castle grow and deciding which direction – literally and figuratively – to go. The game pulls you on the dual axes of your secret endgame VP cards as well as the room completion benefit. That is, when you complete a room, you get a bennie for the type of room it is such as take another turn, get another endgame VP card, etc. Placing adjacent to other rooms may confer a benefit or suffer a penalty; no surprises here. 

Each turn the start player (Master Builder) decides how to order the buildings in the low-to-high pricing row. Leftover buildings from previous turns get a $1 subsidy placed on them which accumulate. The conceit of the game is that the MB receives the money paid by the other players, and since there is no income phase, you rely on this element to keep your cash flow flowing flowingly. You gotta work for your money in this phase.

The weakest part of the game is in how long it might take the current start player to order the building prices. As the game goes on, those considerations get more important and the play can get held up in periods of AP as the MB needs to ensure they’re not giving anybody any cheap points and they're getting themselves as much money as they can.

Complicating matters is even if players take care in how they price buildings, players accumulate more secret goals during the game often rendering those decisions null anyway. I’d much prefer that the auction just happened (or the MB got to swap just two) and players just got on with it.

RATING: N/A
I love, love, love:
- the artwork
- the sense of humor about the king's over-the-top castles
- the decision-filled construction planning
- the pretty and nonsensical castle I end up with at the end of the game

I dislike:
- the many "hidden" points--you also get points for rooms that came from now-empty piles at the end of the game and from having extra money left over!
- how much I end up disliking the other master builders

Final verdict: It's a cute tile-laying game 1) with a theme that seems tacked on after the mechanisms were designed and 2) has most of the scoring gains happening after the game is over. I dislike the latter especially because throughout the game, I see myself getting not-very-many points, but once the game is over, I'm suddenly up another 30, which leaves me with the strangest feeling because it's sudden, kinda extreme, and befuddling. I much more prefer a game where I'm making a lot of point gains throughout, and then at the end, I get just a few more to edge out my opponents rather than getting 20-some points during the game, then all of a sudden 30 more points after the game is over. Right?

RATING: 8.3
Castles of Mad King Ludwig is a really fun game, with an interesting combination of an auction-like Master Builder mechanic, a fun aspect of comboing different points and abilities, and a neat spacial puzzle of how to lay out your castle.  I find the game most fun and strategic at two players, where you are able to really watch what your opponent is doing and price rooms accordingly.  My only complaint is that the rules as written include too few tiles in the two player game - at 11 tiles per player, and many tiles outright removed, you can unknowingly pursue an impossible strategy because the tiles simply aren't in the game.  When played with 26, or even 33 tiles, I find that the two player game is a lot more fair and balanced.

RATING: 10
Players develop castles with different types of rooms  depending what is flavour of the month with the King (what bonus cards are on offer).  Each player takes it in turn to set the price of the rooms.  For two to four players and it works very well with any of these numbers.  
Good for sociable players or families who are happy to play in a semi-co-operative spirit, helping other players to choose which rooms to buy and where to place it in their castle.
The rules leaflet is well written with a lot of amusing flavour text.
The scoring is a little fiddly as players score a couple of VPs for a large range of actions during their turn, and it is best to go slowly and take a benevolent attitude to forgotten VPs.
Perhaps not one for the deeply serious or highly competitive gamer but for the more sociable, a great game.

------------------------------------------------------------
Update: 7-11-16

The more I think about this game, the less I enjoy it. My win percentage is very low. I haven't nailed down the reason, but there isn't any other game I own that I have this much trouble. I guess I can attribute it to bad luck: the way the tiles come out, which bonus cards I'm dealt, or the master builder element altogether. It just always seems I come up short of having a really great game, while other players coast along. I realize I shouldn't complain about my poor strategy in a review, but it does lessen the desire to play when you don't win.  I think I'm going to check out Suburbia instead. Seems a little less luck involved in that one. I really want to keep giving this one a chance, but at the same time I don't.

------------------------------------------------------------
Update: 8/5/16:

After receiving and playing Suburbia, Suburbia is the much better game of the two. I feel like it's more balanced than Castles of MKL. No master builder element to content with, 2-player games feel just the right length (not too short like this game.) For those of you frustrated with this one and haven't tried Suburbia, try that one instead. You may agree with me.

RATING: 7
Essentially a Suburbia clone in its base game mechanics and atmosphere (by the same guy, which is why I bought it). Good for when you want to play Suburbia, but don't want to play Suburbia.

Pros:
1) There's enough deviance from the original Suburbia game mechanics that it's its own game. The Master Builder mechanic is a nice touch.

2) It takes a few times reading it to understand Adjacent vs. Connected, but once you've got that down it's basically Suburbia without the headache of many adjustments to your gameboard just from placing 1 tile, while still retaining the fun of planning the placement of your tiles for adjacency bonuses.


Cons:
1) Didn't know this on the first play, but you won't see all of every stack of tile. This has consequences for your Bonus points at the end of the game (I had 2 bonus cards for get 1 of every room type, and 1 of every room size. Didn't get either because 1 wasn't possible, and somebody else took the other). I might house rule this after another couple of plays.


Overall: Good game, worth playing and replaying. I prefer Suburbia, but this is good for a change of pace in your tile placement games.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.75

I simply love this game, it's a truly gem!

RATING: 7
A Suburbia tervezőjének új játéka, ami első ránézésre sokban hasonlít az elődre, csak itt nem várost építünk, hanem egy kastélyt. Nem uncsi hexákat pakolgatunk, hanem különféle méretű és formájú termeket, folyosókat, kerteket.
Nagyon röviden a játékmenet.
- minden körben van egy master builder, aki feltölti a szobakínálatot, valamint megmondja, hogy melyik szoba mibe kerüljön.
- ezután a többi játékos mindegyike vesz egy szobát, annak árát - most jön a csavar! - a master buldernek kifizetve.
- végül a master bulder is vesz valamit a maradékból, a banknak fizetve.

A szobák lerakása hasonló, mint a suburbiában a városrészeké. Minden szoba ad valami bónuszt, illetve hatása lehet a vele szomszédos szobákra. Pl. egy táncterem ad jó sok pontot önmagában, de elég sok mínusz jár érte, ha egy hálót építünk mellé.
Ami újdonság még, hogy külön bónusz jár egy-egy szoba "befejezéséért", ami nem más, mint az, hogy minden létező ki/bejáratát lezártuk márik szobákkal.

Azt gondoltam elsőre, hogy minek kiadni ugyanazt a játékot más tematikával, de tévedtem. Ebben a játékban máshol vannak a hangúlyok, mint az elődben. Ez leginkább a master bulderes beárazós mechanizmusnak köszönhető, ami legalább olyan fontos, mint maga az építgetés. Mérlegelned kell, hogy mi kell neked, mi kell az ellenfeleknek. Szeretnél minél több pénzt kiszedni a többiekből, de az se jó, ha minden jó cucc elérhetetlenül drága, mert akkor elviszik a kicsit rosszabbat olcsón és nem kapsz elég pénzt. Nomeg mit csinálj azzal a szobával ami neked kéne nagyon? Tedd olcsóvá, de akkor elviszik előled. Legyen drága, de akkor neked is sokat kell fizetni érte. Nem egyszerű. Ez a rész a san marco "én felezek-te választasz" részére hasonlít a leginkább. Sajnos ugyanazokkal a kihívásokkal is küzd, nagyon sok a paraméter, sokat lehet gondolkodni rajta, de nem érdemes, mert a többiek elunják magukat. AP-sekkel ne játssz ilyet!

A hangulata szerintem jobb, mint a suburbiának. Nagyon viccesek a szobák, lehet mulatni az elkészült művön, ami itt sokkal látványosabb is köszönhetően a különböző alakú termeknek. Ez mondjuk egy kicsit hátrány is, mert nem mindig teljesen egyértelmű, hogy mi mivel ér össze, mi hol fér még el. Nagyon precízen kell pakolgatni.

Nagy bajban lennék, ha valaki megkérdezné, hogy melyiket választanám magamnak. Mindkettő tetszik, de másért. A suburbia kombósabb, sokkal több az egymásra hatás, de szárazabb, kevésbé látványos. Mad Ludwigban több a móka és az osztogatós rész is nagyon szellemes.

Nálam egy játék után 7.5, kéne. 

--------
Egy kicsit frissíteném a véleményemet: szerintem 4 játékossal nem annyira jó a játék, így inkább három főnek ajánlott csak. Négyesben már olyan sok paramétert kell figyelembe venni az osztogatásnál, hogy vagy letojod az egészet és gyorsan szétpakolod valahogy - kockáztatva, hogy valakinek kint hagysz valami nagyon jót - vagy 5 percig, orrvérzésig gondolkodsz, ami a többieknek unalmas, és neked se pihentető és élvezetes annyira.
Szóval én azt mondom, hogy ha négyen vagytok, akkor inkább suburbia.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Conceptually and mechanically, Castles of Mad King Ludwig is pretty closely related to its "predecessor" Suburbia. Players draft building parts in order to score combinatorial or topological bonuses while shooting for several secret and/or shared public goals at the same time.

Ludwig is the more free-form of the two games, swapping the hex grid approach of Suburbia for a much less rigid system of connecting rooms by doorways. Many seem to view this change as Ludwig's biggest improvement over Suburbia but I'd say it's its biggest drawback instead. Suburbia's flow is much better whereas Ludwig is constantly interrupted by the master builder having to readjust prices and people trying to fit the pieces into their abodes. Ludwig provides the better sandbox for people to play in but as a game I'd rather stay with Suburbia.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I've really enjoyed every session I've played of this game, even though I don't think I've won once, or even come close.

On the one hand, building your castle is a joy and the end result is just so satisfying to behold.

On the other hand, playing to win requires you to compromise your architectural vision and often leads to a compromised castle design.

WHO CARES? This is one of the only games I've ever played where the outcome of a given session has not affected my mood at all. Win or lose, I am a happy man, over here in the corner, admiring my little castle.

For folks who aren't scrubs like myself, this is a much more competitive game than Suburbia, which I think suffers too greatly from runaway leader syndrome.

RATING: 8
The gameplay is excellent. I like the master builder roll and the favor tiles really change up the different play throughs of the game.

The art is not very good (art is not supposed to be functional, it is supposed to look good) and the Polish version getting updated art so soon to the release is a slap in the face. Won't be purchasing another Bezier product in the future.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.25
Nothing very orignal in the gameplay of this tile laying game.  Players buy tiles after the start player "cuts the cake slices" so to speak, by placing price tags on the available rooms. You can get bonuses actions or points for completing rooms.  The game feels chaotic as the start player will undoubtedly favor one player over another inadvertently --the prices are just too clumsy (low are too low, high are too high except perhaps on the last turn). As a light tile-laying game, however, it works fine --it only becomes chaotic when competing heavily for score. 

Played again and increased rating slightly.  If you accept this game for what it is and just enjoy your castle building puzzle, it's fine.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
So, I have mixed feelings about this game. What I like:  Building the castles is really fun, and while it is not a competitive strategy, I do enjoy trying to make my castle pretty and functional.

That said, the point scoring is a bit boring. About halfway through the game, I start to get a little bored with it... so many room choices, so many incomplete rooms, and it gets tedious to have to choose the highest point scorer among the options available...and then there's the sense of randomness of those choices. You are not really building an engine here. I think that's what I dislike most. Each round is a distinct opportunity to simply get the most points in that round. That means there's no long range planning and it also means that you are basically doing the same thing over and over, and there really isn't that much going on in the game.  

I think the theme is what saves the game and makes it fun. It's FUN to build the castles.

Update:  After playing the game a few times, here's what I think. If I just focus on the aesthetics of building my castle and trying to make it pretty, and don't worry about winning, then I enjoy this game. If I angst over every choice and do endless calculations in in effort to maximize points and win, then I don't enjoy this game. So this may be a first for me: a game I enjoy more when I'm not trying to win.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
For as much as it's like Suburbia, it feels different enough to me that I don't really think about them together.  Obviously you have to compare them, and I think Suburbia is a more strategic game, where this feels more streamlined.  A lot less math in this one, which I'm sure appeals to people.

The main thing that I like about this, vs Suburbia, is it's just less fiddly.  No moving counters, or anything like that.  What I like about Suburbia that is lacking in this is the "plan" aspect, and really looking ahead and planning out how you are going to construct your suburb.  I think you often have to just make the best choice with what is in front of you in this game.  You don't have to temper growing too fast, maintaining cash flow, etc.  

I like it.  I liked it enough to buy it even though I already have Suburbia.  I think it feels "lighter" than Suburbia, and more apt to get non-gamers into.

UPDATE FOR SECRETS EXPANSION:

The expansion is okay.  Sort of an instabuy for me just because of how much CMKL got the the table, but it didn't do a ton for me.  I liked that it didn't feel like I was playing a totally different game.  You definitely have to think about whether people are going to trade the swans in for cash when you price stuff, but I don't know that it added enough to the game to really make me say you need to play with it.  The extra tiles are great, but I could do without the moats.  Just sort of okay in my opinion.

RATING: N/A
Surprised this is rated so highly. Played like an overly complicated version of Isle of Skye. When setting prices/placing tiles in Isle of Skye, you have 4 things to keep track of in a round (current scoring conditions and the end game scrolls), and maybe if the scoring conditions for the other rounds. In Castles, you have at minimum 7 and usually more scoring conditions to keep track of each time you place a room. I don't think adding those extra options brings anything to the game but instead slows it down as you have to consider. Isle of Skye does a better job of making the price setting/market experience feel more exciting too.

I also had big hopes for the theme of this game as everyone talks about how whacky and nice it was. Maybe it's just a product of being produced in 2014 compared to now, but to me it was equally as bland as anything else. The names of some of the rooms were funny ("Mold Room" - where my crazed kingdom stored all its mold), but not any more than the premise of Isle of Skye being building a whisky empire. 

So, clearly I have a preference for the more streamlined, more interactive, more emotional Isle of Skye. This was fun enough to play but it still loses out.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This feels less mathematical than Suburbia, more surprising, and has a more amiable personality. There is probably less opportunity for planning in Castles, where most decisions are made on the fly. But this is compensated by the wonderful system wherein each player in turn gets to arrange the tile market and collect the proceeds. I also enjoy the odd contortions that emerge from the variety of tile sizes and shapes.

Players who don't like this spatial element probably won't enjoy the game. Players who don't like games about making the best of a bad/random situation should also steer clear. The luck of the draw is high here, although there are a lot of opportunities to generate your own serendipity by staying flexible and not playing yourself into a corner (literally).

RATING: 5.5
Fine tile-laying game where each player builds their own castle. The various shapes and sizes add some novelty, but they don't make much of a difference in the end. You make some small combos here and there, try to complete rooms (connecting all doors) and compete for the king's favors (majority bonuses), but there isn't much room for clever moves. Also, every round, one player sets the prices of all available tiles, which is not particularly interesting and severely slows down the pace. And luck of the draw is too high for the amount of thinking required.
