RATING: 7
Tile-laying and price setting game with an interesting theme although it's pretty abstract. Trying to decipher how to get more money from other players as the master builder is the most interesting aspect of the game. You always have to work with imperfect information because of the hidden bonus tiles, but still it's possible to try to estimate what each player is willing to pay for certain tiles. It's a bit fiddly (I made an insert which makes the setup a breeze) and keeping track of other players' castles can be a pain because the text and symbols on tiles is small. (12.12.16)

I've cooled a bit on Castles and hence I'm lowering it's rating to 7 (from 8). The pricing can feel, and be, complete guesswork because you can't know other players' bonus tiles. It's rewarding though when you get it right and reap the rewards. The scoring is also super salad-y so teaching the game is a bit of pain even though the basic mechanisms of price setting and tile laying are easy to grasp. (31.8.17)

RATING: 7.25
Castles of Mad King Ludwig is a reimagining of the Tile laying/City building Suburbia where players build a Castle by combining rooms.

Unlike Suburbia, income is primarily generated from other players during an auction for these rooms, with players taking turns to set prices on the available rooms.

Rooms, like the buildings in Suburbia, appear randomly over time, but their values can increase or decrease over time.

My thoughts: I love the concept, and I am quite fond of Suburbia, even with the luck of the tiles involved in the market- it plays well with any number.

In Castles I find that the game varies more on player count: in a 2-player game, the shorter availability of tiles based on random cards is beginning to annoy. Also, because the income is based on turns as Master Builder or skipping turns altogether, there seems less control over income flow- and accordingly any error in the room valuation process, even one which a player might be blind to, is painful.

This also means that bonuses may not even apply (room type didn't come out, room types not exhausted). All this limits a players ability to stack up points and bonuses, especially considering cash flow with only one other player.

3 players improves that somewhat, giving decent variability in tiles but not quite so random- 75% of tiles will come out, and players get income from two bidders as Master Builder.

In the 4 player game most if not all rooms will come out, and players will have a much more controlled shot at achieving a certain strategy. Of course there is a longer time between income collection rounds, so one must budget accordingly, but adding players to Castles seems to control some of the chaos of budgeting, and provide more alternatives if other players grab up the best rooms.

An expansion, like the one planned in 2015, may add enough tiles to the mix to create more randomness, or add other abilities to raise money or to lure buyers away from some rooms that it increases a Builders chances of slipping their room through the bid at a fair price.

I can't start this as highly as I rate Suburbia, but based on the 3-player and 4-player games I'm rating this a 7.25, for a purely 2-player game I think it would fall to a straight 7. In other words, it is generally somewhat above a standard good game for the 3-4 player counts, but for 2 it falls to a more average rating for me.

The box is very nice, the tiles are a little slender for me, but certainly work. The artwork is good, if not stunning, and like Suburbia there's plenty of extra room in the box for more goodies when expansions come out.

RATING: 8
(06/01/2015) Comment based on one play.

A different twist on the Suburbia building mechanism, where players haphazardly create unique, quirky, and interesting castle layouts from various rooms, hallways, and stairs. The types of rooms come up in a pool randomly, but are priced by the active player from least expensive to most expensive; the rooms are then purchased by the other players with the proceeds going to the active player, and then the active player getting last pick, in a variation of the "I cut, you choose."

Players start out building out their castles from a forum, attaching hallways and adjacent rooms by connecting doorways. Added rooms score a range of VP depending on the specific type of room, then score VP from adjacent rooms depending on compatibility, and then score VP from rooms elsewhere in the castle depending on specific requirements. For example, a bedroom may score 3 VP when placed, but if placed next to a noisy music hall may lose 2 VP. Some rooms are marked underground, where stairs must be placed first before the room can be attached. Rooms don't necessarily need to be placed door-to-door, but through connected hallways. In this manner, a rather haphazard castle layout forms, and at the end of the game bonus points are awarded for four randomly determined public objectives (just like Suburbia). For example, having the most square-shaped rooms, or having the most largest rooms, and so forth. Points are also awarded from hidden bonus cards that players collect throughout the game for building utility rooms.

Similar to Suburbia, the interactions of the various rooms with each other can be daunting at first, but far more manageable. Also, players need not worry about triggering other players' layouts as they do in Suburbia. Still, it is a daunting task to try to optimally price the various rooms, as the active player takes into account what room(s) specific players are interested in, and could pose AP. It turns out that this divide-and-choose mechanism, which happens every turn with every player (as the first-player token moves around the board), generates substantial downtime that can be frustrating to some. Since there are hidden bonus cards and secret motivations, as well as keeping track of the public objectives, is it simply impossible to price every room correctly. As with Suburbia, I frequently reached the point of "I don't care," and arbitrarily assigned prices to the various rooms, just to keep the game moving. Other players would reach similar situations, and thus make suboptimal pricing. Depending on seat position from the active player, this could give the immediate player to the left a substantial VP "gift" because the active player did not consider all of the room interactions. I kept making suboptimal pricing and practically gave the win to the player to my left. Thus, the game can suffer from left-handed player binding.

The game has the potential for interesting combinations, where if a player "completes" a room, by connecting every doorway of the room to some other room or hallway, and ensuring a traceable path to the outside (much like the rooms in Vegas Showdown), the player gets a bonus (be it VP, money, bonus cards, extra turn, etc.) depending on the room type. For example, if a food/dining room is completed, the player gets a second action. If a utility room is completed, they get another hidden bonus card, and so forth. This encourages players to set up rooms in advance for completion, so as to get this bonus which can be very powerful.

I enjoyed this game, which is from the same designer who designed Suburbia, more so because of the interesting combinations and simpler room interactions. However, most in my group did not like the downtime that comes about from the divide-and-choose mechanism. The mechanism is novel and seems essential to the feel of the game, yet it is a source of frustration both from a time and binding standpoint. Perhaps with more plays and familiarity with all of the rooms is essential to speed up the play. As it stands, we wish there had been another way to divvy up the rooms in a more expedient manner. Still, seeing the various castles develop was great fun, and the end game scoring was full of surprises from both the public and secret objectives. It is a "7", for it is far more accessible and playable than Suburbia, but still has some frustrating sharp edges to navigate. We may revisit this one soon.

(08/16/2016) Update after a bunch of unlogged plays. The game has grown on me over the past year, especially due to our coming up with a [thread=1383735]five-player variant[/thread]. For awhile, it was our go-to game whenever we had five players. Somehow, having that fifth player added an additional element of fun, because it was even more nerve-wracking to consider the psychologies of four other players instead of three! Comments abound that it is "better" (read easier and more enjoyable) than Suburbia, because one doesn't have to track the exceptions generated by the other players' tableaus. AP can be a problem when divvying up the rooms, so the group needs to nip such tendencies in the bud early on when learning the game. I'll raise it to an "8", as it is a fun exercise in psychology and building castles!

Este juego lo he jugado yo solo en la app y en el tablero con 4 jugadores. El juego es bueno en ambas versiones. El detalle en contra es que a veces resulta un poco complicado explicar los bonos por completar los cuartos a los demás jugadores, y aún un poco más difícil el explicar y llevar el seguimiento de los cuartos y sus conexiones al colocarlos.

RATING: 8
OH GOD THE SIMPLE ART, THE THIN CARDBOARD IT'S ALL SO...  SO...

[i]Perfectly sufficient.[/i]

If the art were busier it'd be harder to suss out the relevant info on each tile; if the card were thicker nothing would shuffle or stack neatly.  Was it done on the cheap?  I dunno—but it all works [i]beautifully[/i].  Nothing in the entire experience—visual, tactile or otherwise—felt "less-than".

[b]After 10 plays:[/b]  7 --> 8  The game gets crazy-tight when played with experienced people who know what they're doing.  Definitely gets the bump when everyone knows how to properly value rooms based on relative player positions, seating order, and ever-morphing groupthink.

Speaking of which, the orginal art is more than sufficient—it's necessary.  To play competitively you need to be able to take in your opponents' castles at a glance from across the table in questionable lighting—[i]how many pink rooms does she have?  How many food rooms are in play, and where?[/i]  And so on.  Doing this without squinting, or asking (which gives the game away), or [i]getting it wrong[/i] is one of the keys to winning.  The original art facilitates this brilliantly.

[microbadge=33713]

I love the meta that the completion reward for Sleeping rooms is the one that grinds the game to an utter halt.  Really puts the "AP" in "nap time".

[microbadge=33713]

If any tile is overpowered, it's stairs.  I mean, when His Highness is drunk, and tottering at the top step, I'm overcome with terrible urges... [i]to drown him in the shallows of Lake Starnberg.[/i]  My boot upon his neck, bubbles obscuring his startled face, the scrabbling hands.  I see these things as clearly as the flagellation I shall subject myself to in The Hole this evening.

RATING: 7.44
規則:
1.根據人數選擇版塊(4人 4 favor circle)幾個玩家放幾個favor tile
Bonus卡全用 
2.兩人22房間卡，移除2階梯4走廊 移除各2大房 各4小房
三人33房間卡，移除1階梯2走廊 移除各1大房 各2小房
四人44房間卡
3.打開 5\6\7房間卡 (2\3\4 p)，根據卡片抽版塊
4.隨便選起始玩家，分數起始0 其它遞增一分，玩家起始3 bonus卡選一張放回牌庫底，15000元
5.每回合由主要建築師(每輪換人)根據缺幾棟建築物抽卡補版塊，並分配(兩人2000格不用)，如果沒有對應卡片的建築版塊，在繼續抽牌，由主建築師左邊開始挑，每人可選擇付錢買版塊，或pass拿5000，付錢給主建築師，主建築師付錢給銀行，沒拿的版塊上面放1000，上面的錢可以拿來付版塊
6.最後一輪卡不夠抽，先抽完補完，全洗在補抽不夠的
7.最後記分，有資格的拿favor tile記分，各自bonus卡記分，有用完(版塊上空即可)的房間版塊，每塊兩分，包括走廊跟階梯，每10000一分，平手坪數大的勝利
8.每間房間如果出入口都接上，會有bonus動作

------------遊戲設置:
1.主圖板根據人數設置，房間卡2人22張，3人33張，4人44張，洗混朝下放置在卡片區。獎勵卡洗混巢下放置。
2.樓梯與迴廊區:3人移除1樓梯與2迴廊，2人移除2樓梯與4迴廊。
3.房間板塊設置，3人遊戲(350~600)個移除一個，(100~300)移除兩個， 2人遊戲(350~600)個移除二個，(100~300)移除四個
4.房間卡翻開5 / 6 / 7 ( 2 / 3 / 4 人遊戲) 張卡片，依序補充房間
5.起始玩家0分，依序遞增一分。
6.每個玩家一個門廳、3 張獎勵卡，以及 15000 元。按照玩家順位，由建築大師開始，每個玩家把 1 張獎勵卡放到牌庫底部。
---------遊戲流程:
1.建築師分配房間價錢，由建築師左手邊開始購買，錢支付給建築師，亦可購買迴廊或樓梯(3000元)，Pass拿5000元。 未被購買的房間板塊，等結束後每個補1000元。
----------房間放置規則:
01 新房間至少要有一個入口和你的城堡中其中一個房間的入口相接。
02 房間板塊不能彼此交疊。
03 房間可以任意旋轉 90 度 (圓形的房間沿著 90 度的邊上會有入口)。
04 房間多的入口可以和牆相接但必須要滿足至少一入口相接。
05 你的城堡必須總是至少要有一個外部入口 (而不是只有一個會通往封閉空間的入口)。
06 地下房間及地下迴廊只能透過樓梯與地上房間連接。地下房間可以和地上房間相鄰 (牆與牆相觸)，但是地下房間及地下迴廊的入口不能和地上房間的入口相接 (見地下房間的章節說明)。
07 戶外房間的頂端邊緣 (圍欄) 不能相鄰任何房間。
-----------符號種類
1.隔牆相鄰(扣分) 
2.入口相接(加分)
3.地下室種類房間加分
---------房間放置後計分
1. 獲得房間左上角城堡符號的分數。
2. 根據房間板塊中央的符號加分或扣分。
3. 如果你新放置的房間有符合條件，檢查和你新放置房間的相鄰房間上，是否有可以讓你加分或扣分的符號。
4. 檢查任何的地下房間，看你新放置的板塊是否有符合它們「每個你的某種房間」符號並加分。
5. 如果你完成一個或多個房間，得到這些房間的完成獎勵。
--------------房間完成獎勵一覽
1.客廳:再次計分該房間的分數 (左上角以及中央符號的分數)。
2.活動房間:立刻獲得一次額外的 5 分。
!!!!!3.睡眠房間:察看一種尺寸的任一疊房間板塊，並把 0、1，或 2 個這種尺寸的板塊放到房間卡牌庫上方。把該疊剩餘的房間板塊洗混後正面朝下放回原位。這些選定的房間會在下一輪一開始要填補合約圖板下方的空格時，按順序把最後放的房間板塊先抽出來填補。
4.戶外房間:得到10000
5.工具房間:從獎勵卡牌庫拿取兩張獎勵卡，保留一張並把另一張正面朝下放到牌庫底部。
6.餐廳: 立刻多一動，買房間、迴廊、樓梯或拿5000。
7.迴廊房間(樓梯也算): 拿取一個迴廊或樓梯
8.地下房間:每兩個地下房間，可以選擇七選獎勵。
-------------遊戲結束
1.若有一輪房間卡牌庫不夠補充，這該輪為最後一輪。建築師洗混剩下棄牌卡確保最後一輪完整房間。
2.城堡中對於每個「已耗盡房間板塊」的房間 (包含迴廊和樓梯) 可以獲得 2 分，每10000元1分。
3.中間國王的獎賞分數:分為a.方形圖示(比較坪數)  b.圓形圖示(比較間數) ，分數平手的加總取平均(向下取整)

RATING: 8
This game inevitably gets compared to Suburbia, but the games only share a common mechanic of tile selection and placement.  I enjoy this one quite a bit as the theme of castle building grabs me over civic planning.  The castles that get constructed are indeed "mad," as room type and function take a back seat to the scoring selections.  It can feel on the short side at times as each castle will only consist of 10 or so rooms, depending on how often you are forced to forgo buying a room and instead get some free coin.  I really like the Master Builder mechanic, which is a take on the "I-split-you-choose" concept.

RATING: 8.5
Looking at the cheap components and uninteresting artwork, I didn't expect to like this game. Hearing the rules explanation I didn't expect to like this game. But CoMKL really surprised me. It's a very satisfying puzzley game as you try to maximize points while building your castle, and it kept me engaged the entire time. Only downside is the possible swingy nature of the scoring, especially if - as in Puerto Rico - the current Builder player makes a suboptimal play such as charging a low price for a building that scores someone else massive points because they weren't paying attention - unfortunately such moves, and they will happen, tend to punish all players and reward one due to someone's mistake. I suppose it's part of the gameplay, but I think that combined with the poor production quality keep this from going higher in my rating. I'd never turn down a game, but I wouldn't buy it myself.

RATING: 7
A challenging puzzle, attempting to get as many victory points as possible from collecting the right types of rooms and arranging them next to their optimal counterparts. Hidden objectives and your opponents changing the cost of rooms on whim all serve to keep you interested. My biggest quibble with the game is that the theme doesn't shine through as strongly as I'd hoped. Rarely do you stop to consider what the actual rooms are and how they are connected as you are more focussed just on their types. Some bolder artwork and more mechanics that engage with the actual name of the room might evoke a bit more charm out of this decent castle building game.

RATING: 6
:thumbsup: aesthetically ancient

An excellent game with it's main mechanism based around the pie-cutting problem that is deeper and less random than Suburbia

2 Players: more strategic than tactical = my preferred way to play
4 Players: more tactical than strategic = analysis paralysis downtime chaos party

I've replaced the coins with poker chips to make it easier to stack tiles on top of them to identify the discount total without obscuring important information of the room and simultaneously making the game less fiddly and tactilely enjoyable.

One minor nitpick about the graphic design is that the symbols distinguishing whether you gain points for connecting a certain room type to another, or adding a certain room type to your castle could be clearer. The upstairs room graphics should read "# of points in white text with a black bold outline(without the castle icon underneath) / symbol for connected to doorway / color and symbol of room type" to signify scoring bonus points for rooms of that type connected to [b][i]this[/i][/b] room. The downstairs room graphics should read "# of points in white text with a black bold outline(without the castle icon underneath) / color and symbol of room type with the castle icon underneath the room type icon" to signify you gain points for each of those room types [b][i]in[/i][/b] your castle.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Great game.

Having to reconcile different requirements - coming from 3 different sources: common goals, personal goals and bonuses from rooms being closed - when purchasing/placing a part of the castle is superb.  These requirements are often not aligned well (or plain contradictory) or they are tangled with each other or some overlapping requirements are competition to the other set of overlapping requirements etc etc.
The cash is tight, the prices of castle parts change constantly and there are other factors to consider too when expanding your castle...

Tough choices and LOTS of fun.

The similiarity to Suburbia is insignificant and meaningless.  CoMKL is much richer, interesting and exciting. I hate Suburbia and I love Castles of Mad King Ludwig.

EDIT: AVOID the Polish version.
  Nice graphics, but COMPLETELY unreadable.  If you want to play comfortably buy yourself an album on castles and the game in English version.

RATING: 8
Castles of Mad King Ludwig has some resemblance to the designer's Suburbia, but it sacrifices the engine building for a heavier emphasis on tactical and spatial gameplay. And it's a better game for it.

There's much to appreciate here: the cleverness of allowing one player to set the market while allowing others to choose rooms first, the many paths and points combinations that can lead to victory, and the chuckles from the crazy castle layouts players make, to name a few. It's a solid choice for players who want a spatial placement game.

RATING: 5
2015-01-28

Initial Rating: 6.5 (January 2015)

Many friends told me that Castles of Mad King Ludwig is very Suburbia derivative and as I don’t really like Suburbia I did my best to avoid this game. However I’m glad a friend made me play it as I really liked it. Fun theme.

It is true that Castles of Mad King Ludwig shares a lot with Suburbia, the core is the same, however the changes are for the best. I really like that there is a master builder each turn and that he arranges the available rooms as he sees fit, this “I split you choose” like mechanism is amazing, it improves the game a lot for me but it can cause AP, so I think the game is best with 2 or 3 players.

The components are very well done and when you assemble your castle, the rooms look gorgeous on the table. I’m not really a fan of hidden goals because they add a lot of luck that cannot be controlled in this game.

Bottom line, I enjoy Castles of Mad King Ludwig. I want to play again and perhaps I will buy it. For me it is way better than its predecessor Suburbia. 

Current Rating: 6.5

RATING: 7
While Suburbia is reglemented by comprehensive building regulations, King Ludwig suspended these regulations and opted for free-form construction. In a variation of the pie-split mechanism, the active player decides on the respective price for each available tile but has the last choice from the offer. The differing shapes and the corridors ensure that no two castles will look the same.
While sufficiently different from Suburbia, both games share enough similarity and fill the same niche that I don't need both. Visually, Suburbia is more appealing and it plays quicker although the administrative part has a higher share. I stay with Suburbia but enjoy King Ludwig as well.

RATING: 7

Optimized by the fact that each building is worth a different amount to each player, thanks to all those synergies. The ability to close rooms, and the bonuses they give you, means that you can have different strategies.

Recently replaced by Isle of Skye, which offers 90% of the fun and decision in 50% of the time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
This game was clearly inspired by Avalon Hill's Vegas Showdown.  It's as close as I think I've ever seen a highly successful game copy something else.  Of course Vegas Showdown was begging to be copied since it's so unique and not without its design issues.  I do think this game managed to correct most of what I didn't like about Vegas Showdown while keeping everything I did (And it's certainly managed to evolve into something entirely its own at the same time!).  The components are high quality by comparison and the individualized rooms and art detail are superb.  The bonus cards in Vegas Showdown were far too randomized compared to the cards here.  The way money works in Castles of Mad King Ludwig is a bit thematically odd feeling though since you pay your money to the start player which rotates each turn.  I ditched Vegas Showdown years ago despite absolutely loving the building half of the game.  I'm pretty confident Castles of Mad King Ludwig could earn a permanent place in our collection.

Beyond the thematic weirdness of paying money to the current start player vs. the bank my main remaining "minor" issue is in how the start player can rearrange the order and therefore price of the rooms currently up for sale.  It's a very AP prone task made tougher by often limited information as to what to really price how.  Left to little thought it can swing the game pretty good so I cannot help but wonder if it wouldn't have just been a better design to straight out just have unbought titles slide down.  Our last game I only won by 3 points and I cannot help but wonder if that was mostly just due to an opponent NOT spending enough time analyzing and pricing the tiles.  Certainly seems like the weight of game where you would not want to encourage AP in order to win.  That all said I suppose I personally prefer the rules as-is as I'm an experienced enough gamer to decide quickly and it does present another meaningful decision and a bit of "push your luck" in attempting to place the tile or tiles your really after in such a way as they might be left for you yet still as affordable as possible.

RATING: 9
Christmas, 2014

Castles is by far my favorite tile-laying game (a type of game I typically find boring). It features cool and quirky rooms you can add to your castle, and a clever "Master Builder" role that allows the players to take turns setting the prices for all the rooms (and hopefully profiting from it). The scoring takes a little getting used to, but once you understand how it works the game is a blast to play. It's not a heavy game, but this game comes out frequently in a group of gamers who love heavy games. It's that good!

RATING: 3
[size=12][b]Top 300 or higher board games list[/b][/size]

[size=12][b]Why I like Castles of Mad King Ludwig:[/b][/size]
+ I like the theme of building a castle
+ I like the mechanics
+ There is decent player involvement
+ There is decent replayability
+ The game is easy to teach
+ The game duration is alright

[size=12][b]Why I dislike Castles of Mad King Ludwig:[/b][/size]
- I dislike the master builder mechanic because you do not know what other players want and hence struggle to make a meaningful decision in regards to pricing the tiles. The tile pricing phase also causes the game to go for a longer duration. 
- The artwork for the castle could be better. 
- Suburbia is too similar to this game and Suburbia works better for 2 players.

[size=12][b]VERDICT:[/b] Not willing to play this game[/size]

RATING: 5
I'm not a big fan of the "I cut the pie, you choose the slices" mechanism any games, as it always leads to the same problems (of which plenty of downtime is probably the worst). Other than that Castles of Mad King Ludwig is an ok game. The scoring mechanism(s) isn't the most innovative ever, but building the castle is an ok puzzle with some interesting decisions. Graphics are bland and make playing the game more difficult as it isn't always immediately clear which category a tile belongs to.

RATING: 8
Suburbia is one of my favourite games and wanted more so I got this. I enjoy how they changed the game from a "strategic long term" to "tactical re-active" game. While Suburbia I think is the superior game, this one seems a lot easier to teach to new players (sort of a gateway Suburbia) and I can see this one actually seeing more table time because of that. Great addition to my collection.

RATING: 2
I like building games, but it's hard for me to imagine a game more boring and tedious than this one. 

There is nothing fun about this between the myriad of fussy cardboard tiles and clumsy board pieces, the repeated squinting at a tiny symbols on said tiles, and the heavy "screw your opponent" mechanic to avoiding buying on your non-Master-Builder round. The beginning-of-round room-drafting / re-arranging is dead time. The end-of-round scoring is PAINFUL. 

Seriously: there is not one fun part to this game. I'd honestly rather play War with a deck of cards or wash dishes. Both activities are more entertaining. 

Unrelated to gameplay, a few specific ambiguities in the instructions that seemed like they could have easily been avoided with better editing.

RATING: N/A
In the tile-laying game Castles of Mad King Ludwig, players are tasked with building an amazing, extravagant castle for King Ludwig II of Bavaria...one room at a time. You see, the King loves castles, having built Neuschwanstein (the castle that inspired the Disney theme park castles) and others, but now he's commissioned you to build the biggest, best castle ever — subject, of course, to his ever-changing whims. Each player acts as a building contractor who is adding rooms to the castle he's building while also selling his services to other players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Introduction:
Castles (of Mad King Ludwig) is a tile-placement with a unique auctioning mechanic.  The tiles are rooms and are of various shapes and colors/types.  

Players take turns being the "Master Builder," which means determining the price that each piece can be bought for during the next round.  There are several slots for $2000, $4000, $6000, etc, and he chooses which tile will be in each slot.

The Master Builder Arranging the Prices:
This auction is the most interesting part of the game, because players may either pay the Master Builder the price for a tile, OR they may not buy anything and simply take $5000 that turn.  So the Master Builder is trying to arrange tiles so that they will pay a moderate amount, but not too high, or they may just opt out of paying him at all.  Alternately, he could purposefully put something really high because he doesn't want his opponent to have it.  

And, because the Master Builder goes lost, he's also thinking about the tile(s) that he would like to buy--does he put them low so that he doesn't have to pay much (but risks someone else buying it first), or does he put it high and pay a lot, but be relatively assured that no one else will buy it?

There turns out to be a lot of thought that goes into the master builder arranging the auction, and in the end there's no "right" answer, but there are certainly things that can set up an opponent, and things that can set yourself up for success if you're reading the situation correctly.  It really "pays" (har har) to "pay" (oh please stop) attention to what your opponents are going for.  

Bonuses:
Speaking of which, some goals are public (and everyone is competing for them) and some are secret.  I like that they made a variety of goals instead of them all being the same.  You get bonus points not only for obvious things (like have the most orange tiles or have the most 350-size tiles), but more interesting things like: every open entrance in your castle, every round room, every square room, every completed room, every incomplete room, get a TON of points for having one of every room, get a TON of points for having one of every shape of room...etc.

The names of the room are thematic and sometimes humorous--I always get a kick out of "The Mold Room" and "The Bottomless Pit."

BUT HOW DOES PLACING ROOMS IN YOUR CASTLE WORK?
Glad you asked.  Every time your purchase a tile/room, you must attach at least one of its entrances to an entrance of another room.  Then the room gets you points for building it and bonuses (or penalties) based on what rooms it is next to.  These two things are listed on the tiles so it's easy to see what each tile does.

When you make a move that causes each entrance on a room to be connected to another room, then that room is "completed."  Completing a room gets you a reward based on what color of room it is, and all of these rewards are listed on nice cheatsheets that each player has.  Yellow (food) rooms get you an extra turn, purple (living) rooms re-score their points, green (outdoor) rooms get you $10000, etc.

These completion rewards make rooms with only one or two entrances very desirable as they allow you to quickly get those rewards and catapult to the lead.

Pace/downtime:
The game plays in about two hours.  I will warn that some people can be a little AP when they are the MB (master builder).  It's a very different kind of puzzle to solve and it effects people differently.  Some people feel liberated by it and move things very little, taking 5 seconds.  Others feel like it's hard to find the optimal solution (if you could have such a thing) and spend a lot of time creating an ordering, and then going "but I don't want to give THAT away for 2000" and reordering again.  No matter what, you're going to have to give SOMETHING away for 2000!  By and large though, most people enjoy being the master builder and don't take TOO terribly long.

So this game is "ok" on downtime.  It only plays up to 4 and I think that's a good choice.

Components:
Everything is nice solid cardboard.  The cards are quite small but very readable and they take up less space on the board that way, so I'm ok with the fact that they're harder to shuffle.

The box itself is one of the most beautiful boardgame boxes I've ever seen.  Unfortunately there's nothing in the actual game with artwork like that, so I hope no one comes into it expecting that.

One detail that could use work: the cardboard cutouts that hold all the pieces and have the scoring track.  They don't lie perfectly flat, so they are easily moved and I keep ending up fiddling with them.  That's a small negative.

Gameplay:
The MB (master builder) arranging and auctioning is fairly innovative and offers something that no other game has.  Also, figuring out each turn what you're going to buy is a constantly changing little puzzle and I've found it to be very rewarding.  It feels like I ALWAYS have good choices in this game.  If someone takes the tile that I really wanted, there is oftentimes another tile that can still really help me in different ways, and then there is always the hallways and stairways that I could build this turn, and I can always just use my turn to get money!  

So really, I never feel overly constrained with this game.  Also, it has a really good sense of "agency"--I have something to do with everything that goes into my castle.  There is the random luck of what tiles come out on what turn, but I choose how much I'm willing to pay for things and where to put them and which bonuses I'm going to try to score--short-term and long-term.  Furthermore, I as the MB have a say in what my opponents can do.  I really like that too--direct player interaction that doesn't feel nasty but is definitely meaningful and alters the game.

Luck vs Strategy:
I always try to measure the degree to which a game's outcome is determined by luck.  Then I measure how long the game takes to play, and basically, I don't want the first to be higher than the later.  I don't like games that have the outcome largely determined by luck and are also long.  So how does COMKL do in this category?

Well it's really hard to say.  As far as bonuses, I feel like there's a small degree of luck, because the secret bonuses (of which each player starts off with 2 of) seem pretty well-balanced to me, and then if you don't go after the public bonuses at all then that's just your fault--you knew about them the whole game, and there's no way you can say you didn't have a way to do well on at least one of them if you wanted to.

The part that can be more lucky is about which tiles come out at which times.  So if a bunch of good tiles come out and it's your turn to be the MB (so you're going last), then it may seem like you're having bad luck.  On the other hand, by placing those tiles in the right place, you can rack up on money that round.  And on the other other hand, it's not like there are tiles that are strictly better than others--it all depends on what strategies you're going for.

Now, if you're going for the same thing as your opponent and a good tile for that strategy comes out, AND neither of you has any good alternatives out there (which won't be true 90% of the time)...then maybe it really matters on whose turn that tile comes out.  But you're getting points from so many different sources, that I just don't feel like that's a big deal.

One of my friends (that has played this only once) disagree with me--he felt that luck didn't go in his favor because 1) when he was the MB, people "passed" (took $5000 instead of buying from him) several times so he got less money.  2) He felt like the tiles that were available when he was the MB were not as good as ones on other people's turns.

My opinion is that the second complaint has SOME validity if multiple people go after the same types of tiles...and for the public bonuses, that will be true.  But there are always people going after different secret bonuses, and there's always other reasons for getting something--easy to complete, and the type of completion bonus you'll get, and maybe you have a tile that gives you a bonus for putting that type next to it. 

SO...IMO you can always make out OK if you play the game right.  That's just my opinion.

Theme:
I like that this is a tile placement game where the tiles are not all the same shape, and where we're not just putting them in a grid ("thank the Maker!").  I'm not sure how taking the turns being the MB works out thematically, but hey, maybe because Ludwig is mad, he randomly picks different people to be his favorites?

The basic premise of each castle having different things that you get bonuses for makes a lot of sense to me.  And the bonuses that rooms have for being next to other rooms usually feels very thematic and sensible as well.  There's also a rule about outdoors rooms that has to do with not blocking the view--another great little thematic rule.  

And you have real incentives to create a downstairs as well as an upstairs!  Basically, there are a lot of ways in which I think the gameplay/rules themselves feel thematic, and that's the best kind of theme IMO.

I like that the different rooms have some subtle humor to them.  There's a "bottomless pit" room for instance.  Or a "mold room", which they just made up to be funny (there's no such room in history).  And some of them are actually informative, like the Venus Grotto room.

Replayability:
So far I'm not getting tired of it; we'll see how things progress.  The public bonuses and the secret bonuses are randomized every game and that's the biggest thing that's going to make games feel different.  Also the randomness of what rooms come out when really makes every turn feel unique.  Also several little things change based on number of players that give it a little different feel.

It sounds like the expansion for this is REALLY good so that is also a plus; knowing that I can pick that up for only $30-40 and add on lots more mileage if I get tired of it.  So I give this a solid rating for replayability.

Player Interaction:
Like I said I think this has good player interaction; players can only do moves that the MB allowed them to do when he set up the auctioning!  Strong player interaction without it being just "take that"...THANK YOU!

Rulebook:
The rulebook is fine.  There are several little rules to remember and so I've consulted it many times.  It doesn't usually take very long to figure it out however.

Balanced Strategies:
I do feel like the various strategies are balanced here.  And by strategies I mean: the different secret bonus cards, the different public bonuses, the different types of buildings, and trying to maximize income as the MB vs stopping opponents from doing what they want.  And all of these things could be focused on more than the others, and I think that THAT is balanced too!  As far as I can tell at least.

Final thoughts:
Again, I feel this is innovative, original, I feel the mechanics and bonuses are thematic, and I really like the player interaction.  Strategies seem to be balanced and I am fine with the level of luck for how long this game is.

I honestly don't have any major negatives!  All I have are things that are fairly good but not perfect:
1. It's not QUITE as replayable as it could be
2. Fiddly-ness of the score board which moves around because it's not perfectly flat
3. Some people feel it's got a lot of luck
4. Some people suffer from AP when being the MB
5. Some people feel like we're all playing solitaire (again I think there's lots of player interaction with the MB arranging prices)

I guess the main one thing I don't like is: the rules are not elegant.  There are just a lot of little rules to remember and several things to fiddle with every round.  You inevitable forget to add points for something somewhere, because this is a points-salad kind of game.  And there's just so many little rules.  Many of them are kind of intuitive so that helps--but I still had to consult the rulebook many times on my 4th play.

Also, there is no stellar artwork or anything in this game--besides the box cover.

"Should I get this?"
I don't think this is for everyone.  It's a medium-weight game, and I think it will be too heavy for the very light gamers and too random for some of the heavier eurogamers.  But I think it has a wide appeal for everyone not in those two extremes--unless you're in one of those two categories, I think this is a great buy.

Summary & Rating:
This was really hard to decide, because while there aren't any huge negatives, there is also just something lacking in order to make it truly exciting, like this is one of my favorite games of all time.

Strategic depth:
3/5
Luck vs length:
4/5
Theme:
4/5
Art & component quality:
3/5
Innovation/originality:
4/5
Replayability:
3/5
Extensibility:
4/5
Rules elegance:
2/5
Rulebook/ease of teaching it:
3/5
Player interaction:
5/5
Pace (not being prone to analysis-paralysis):
3/5

Overall I give it an 8/10.  Better than most games I consider "good," and I think I'll be playing this many years into the future.  However, due to too many areas being just "good" (3/5) and not "great", I can't quite rate it as one of the best games of all time.

RATING: 7
This is similar to Suburbia but with differently shaped tiles, the master builder pricing rather than the gradual reduction in price market, and almost everyone gets some points from the public goals rather than one or none.

I don't think I'm a fan of the master builder mechanic. It seems cool in principle, and is somewhat thematic, but it makes it so players need to be fairly experienced to prevent seat order from giving someone an advantage or disadvantage, as a beginner or unassuming player can make a room great for a person to their left really strong.

The non uniform shape of the rooms adds some tricky placement planning, and you can easily accidentally block off doorways, adding another level of strategy.

There are many ways to score via set collection in the game: public goals, depleting all of a room stack to score 2 points for each of your rooms of that type, colors for adjacent rooms and being able to collect hypothetically a near infinite number of private goals. The rooms that score for non adjacent rooms have the same problem as Suburbia in that it is very easy to forget about them (whether your own or other players when pricing for master builder).

Unless you chain through blue rooms like crazy you use (rough estimate) 60% of the tiles per game, so I think variability is better than Suburbia, but I still wish there were more different ones.

If the artwork was more whimsical instead of somewhat downcast and the master builder mechanic was tweaked to be more balanced with players at different levels I think I would like this game more than Suburbia, but as it stands it only gets a high 7.

RATING: 8
A clever game where you are building a castle with different room types.  Room placement is very important and you can get extra points if rooms of complementary type perform well.  And you can be punished if they opposite.  A bedroom by a loud room is not good.  Each room has different special power.

The game is very similar to Suburbia also by Ted Ansbach.  But I like it a little more than Suburbia since the game is less abstract and there is less remembering that other players actions can trigger points and money for you.  

Excellent and game and very much worth playing and owning.

RATING: 7
Pros: I like the theme, seeing the castles get built, and the management of one's personal budget until you get to be master builder again (i.e. get a source of income). The game also scales very well (rare for a game to play well at 2 players and more than 2), though I haven't tried solo yet.

Cons: The convoluted scoring method of this game is too much overhead, and makes me think that the game replaces depth of strategy with obfuscation. Plus it's awkward to try and read all the bonus icons from another player's castle. The point salad nature also encourages analysis-paralysis. I'm also not a fan of the room-stack finishing bonus, which is mostly random.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Suburbia's little brother? Perhaps. Yet, this is the better of the two tile laying games. In fact, for me, it surpasses Suburbia in just about every way. I appreciate a medieval theme more than a city-building theme. The play time is shorter by about 30 minutes. My wife and I can play a game of Castles in about an hour compared to 90 minutes with Suburbia. The master builder is an awesome mechanic that has no match in Suburbia. Also, and perhaps most importantly, the scoring is much less convoluted in Castles. I will say, that despite the arguments on both sides, the tiles are thin in Castles, thin enough to damage one when punching them out (despite being careful). So, it loses a half point in that regard. Nevertheless, the thin tiles do not negatively affect gameplay. Plus, the art on the tiles is very nice. I love this game, and can't wait to play it with more than 2!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
A game I didn't mind losing in for at least 10 or so plays. Heck, I still don't really mind sometimes if my castle is pretty enough.

RATING: 7
Rating raised on further plays. The core game is fine, and my issues with it seem to only affect the 2-player game, but I don't entirely like the bonus cards. It feels like the game can have a huge 'points salad' swing from hidden bonuses at the end - I've seen 29 pts come out of thin air from a player who played averagely and collected bonus cards.

Comments added after playing 3-4 players.

(based on 2-player)
Much-hyped bestseller left me a little cold due to the randomness of the game's scoring potential. It feels like the design fights itself, setting up room combos and scoring objectives which rely on rooms which may never enter play. The other thing is the secret bonus cards may agree or conflict with the open bonuses and the make-up of the room cards, giving players an unfair boost or disadvantage.

Could have been a great game, I'm not sure why it's been designed to subtract from the inherent depth at the game's core. Still a good game to play over a cup of tea - it feels good to build something and growing your castle is an enjoyable process. I think the component quality is perfectly fine, too.

Edit: After playing a 4-player game, I much, MUCH prefer this number of players, in fact it feels like this was designed for 4 and scaled down badly to 2.
Further Edit: 3-players is the sweet spot for me, though I still don't like the bonus cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I found the game to be overly complex and rules heavy for what it was - castle building.  Quite a bit of meat, with room combos and scoring.  Primary interaction mechanism was via the master builder ranking the different room tiles in order.  

I have the same issue with this as Princes of Florence - it relies on players being aware of the relative value of the rooms and paying close attention to other players to see what is going on so that they don't inadvertently give one player a massive advantage (which not only detriments the builder but also the other passive players).  The importance of this role encourages AP big time.  Certainly not a game to be played casually and hence one that I wouldn’t be keen to play again.  For that reason I can't give it more than a 6.

RATING: 9.5
Edit: after more than 20 games in a year now, I can certainly say that this game is my hit for 2014. The extension is not so interesting, but the base game is just WOW

------

Easy game, rules are explained in ~20 minutes.

The material is not beautiful, and could be somewhat more evident: it's sometimes hard for new players to understand where the room symbols are located. After a few game, you quickly get the feeling and everything is now clear. 

The game is incredible!  
The mechanism of having a player being the one who will set the price of the rooms for each round is brilliant and brings the needs for the players to check the castles of other players, even if they can't impact directly the castles (not destruction/interaction possibility). 

In addition of the mecanic, the theme is nice: I have seen a player making a batman cave, building only underground rooms. When I have time (in few downtime), I look at the layout of each castle, having fun in looking at the strange connections between rooms :)

There are few "combos" to make, but you can't break the game with a flow of combos chains, so it's more like you make a good round than going crazy in victory points. 

Have fun!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game is satisfying on many levels.  There's immense enjoyment in simply building your wonky castle.  It can be quite deep, and yet one can play it fairly casually.  The theme comes through with the various room types and names.  Players might even be tempted to explain their castle's layout.

The Master Builder phase, where you set the prices of tiles, can be an AP trap.  However, despite being prone to AP myself, after a couple of plays I'm able to make a decent decision and then "walk away" from the line up.

It's a winner because the theme is fun, and the game is both breezy and deep at the same time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Lighter than Suburbia.  In Suburbia each hex will interact with up to 6 tiles around it:  so there are lots of interactions.  Suburbia also has 3 different "resources" to manage: income, reputation and population.  These features make Suburbia an extremely deep and mathy game.  CML simplifies the resources to just money.  Also, the highly interactive hex-tiles of Suburbia are replaced by eccentric tetris-like shapes which usually interact with only 2 or 3 other tiles.  These changes make CML lighter with less to think about with each choice.  The master builder mechanism of CML is quite fun and the tetris like shapes feel more visually appealing.  Overall, I probably like CML better than Suburbia, but I think a lot of people will like the depth & complexity of Suburbia better.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
First and foremost, not a bad game. I'll gladly play it again. Sadly for Mad King, there's one game out recently, which does nearly the same thing better.

So why do I think Isle Of Skye is superior to Mad King Ludwig?
1) The graphics of game components. Sorry.
2) The theme. I understand this is just my personal preference (my wife and I visited British isles just this autumn, while the castles just remind me of our dreadful high school history teacher :-/ ), but then again, this is my personal game evaluation.
3) IoS can take 5 players.
4) The Mad King tiles don't follow some trivial square grid to be seen. I get it, that's thematic for mad castles, but changing the tile angle or position by less than a millimetre will suddenly make some doors not fit, even though they're supposed to be fitting. That won't happen to you in Skye, with a plain Carcassone-like square network.
5) The four random shared goals are scored just once in Mad King, at the end. For as little as 2 points, this feels kinda anti-climactic.
6) Being a master builder is a HUGE advantage. A *huge* one. That's a normal thing in games, 1st player is somehow better or worse each round than all other players - but if that's the case THIS much, there should be a mechanism making sure that all players have been first the same number of times.

And to be fair, why is Mad King better than Isle of Skye?
1) It has a prettier box. That's where the "prettier" stops.
2) The different little effects triggered when closing a room are one extra nice thing to think about. Sadly they are scarcely a game-changer. But sometimes even little differences matter! :)
3) Examining the function of your built castle might bring some funny talks - "So, to bring the food from kitchen to dining room, the cooks have to go thru catacombs and meditation room. Great..."

TL,DR: Not a bad game. But unless you're a big middle-ages architecture fan, rather go check out Isle of Skye.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Similar in it's design to Suburbia, players will purchase tiles (Rooms) to add to their castle. Certain rooms will synergize better or worse with other rooms (ie. you don't want a music room next to a bedroom). A market row is populated with room tiles, and players take turns acting as a "master builder", where they can set the price of tiles on that row, remembering that when they do this, other players will pay them for the tile they choose, and the master builder themselves will pay the bank. Therefore, you want to ensure you price tiles appropriately to tantalize other players into buying them from you, but not the one you want to buy! Excellent lighter-midweight euro style game with lots of good, tough decision making.

RATING: 7
I didn't think it would, but Castles has replaced Suburbia for me. I still enjoy Suburbia more for the individual tile powers, but I really enjoy the Master Builder mechanism here. It kind of feels like an inverted auction. This one definitely plays better with more though. Suburbia I'd still probably prefer with 2, but Castles is much better with 3 or 4. Really the only issue I have with this game is the setup and storage, which are way more complicated than they should be. For the price of this game, they should have included a decent insert. I also think the hidden scoring cards can be a little overpowered if someone goes heavily into them, but otherwise this is an excellent game, and my favorite tile-laying game to date.

Replaces Suburbia, and is more enjoyable than Isle of Skye.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In this game, themed after Mad King Ludwig, players take turns being a Master Builder to set prices for different rooms. Players purchase rooms, with the cost going to the Master Builder and construct the room into their castle. Closing a room by enclosing all entranceways of the room gives the player a bonus, such as more VPs or another turn. 

At the beginning of the game, 4 out of 24 favour tiles are drawn randomly to determine what will also score points for majority purposes (most of a particular tyoe will score 8 VPs).

It's a fun game, and I want to get in more plays. I understand the flow better after my first game, and there are many ways to earn points. The icons and colors can be a bit tough to understand at first, but it should become second nature. There were a few subtlties that I missed in my first game, and would like to play again to improve. Rating may rise with more plays.

RATING: 6
Dry tile laying game that has an excellent in game pricing system. The master building orders the value of rooms available to build (increasing by set prices) and players must pay the master builder. 

PROS
-Price /value system is great, would like to see this in other games. 

CONS
-Basic match the colours etc tile placing game.
-Minimal to no interaction. 
-Game in general, especially the room names, are too abstract. 
-Luck comes from whether people have money; if you are the master builder and everyone is broke, your income is next to nothing no matter you do.
-Lots of components to sort and stack each game.  
-Keeping score is a painful chore.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Firstly, I hate the box for this. There's no bags it comes with, there's a lot of empty air, the tiles are fiddly, and the cards are SO tiny. Actual gameplay is fiddly with matching up your castle, trying not to bump anything off, and leaving teeny, tiny coins on the rooms to build up annoys me since you have to move them for almost all the master builder's turns. Overall, the components disappointed me.

Gameplay itself is decent, but not very captivating for me personally. I felt there was a lot of downtime when the MB was deciding how to orient it, and even then, sometimes it wouldn't matter, if a bunch of people were gonna get stairs or something anyway. Other times, when you're MB, your decision for what to price at what was random, at least for the start of the game. You could do the sorting by VP count or completion bonus, but overall, its hard to know what your opponent is aiming for in the beginning. Sometimes, you could also really need a certain room type, and in lower player games (2 or 3 player), only 1-3 of the room types come out at all.

Overall, this was a very disappointing game given how much it was heralded in 2014. I suppose the one good thing was that I learned a bit more about my tastes and what games I like and don't like.

Plays: 3+?

RATING: 6
The scoring is not quite as fiddly as it seems based on the rulebook but it still takes a bit more time than what I would be comfortable with. The scoring scheme also directly causes slowing down of other parts of a round: The master builder reordering the tile market, and the players deciding which tile to buy. When all of these downtime-generating phases are combined, the resulting game moves way too slowly for the tile placement puzzle to be worth the time invested.

RATING: 8.5
規則:
1.根據人數選擇版塊(4人 4 favor circle)幾個玩家放幾個favor tile
  Bonus卡全用 
2.兩人22房間卡，移除2階梯4走廊 移除各2大房 各4小房
  三人33房間卡，移除1階梯2走廊 移除各1大房 各2小房
  四人44房間卡
3.打開 5\6\7房間卡 (2\3\4 人)，根據卡片抽版塊
4.隨機起始玩家，分數起始0 其它遞增一分，玩家起始3 bonus卡選一張放回牌庫底，15000元
5.每回合由主要建築師(每輪換人)根據缺幾棟建築物抽卡補版塊，並分配(兩人2000格不用)，如果沒有對應卡片的建築版塊，在繼續抽牌，由主建築師左邊開始挑，每人可選擇付錢買版塊，或pass拿5000，付錢給主建築師，主建築師付錢給銀行，沒拿的版塊上面放1000，上面的錢可以拿來付版塊
6.最後一輪卡不夠抽，先抽完補完，全洗在補抽不夠的
7.最後記分，有資格的拿favor tile記分，各自bonus卡記分，有用完(版塊上空即可)的房間版塊，每塊兩分，包括走廊跟階梯，每10000一分，平手坪數大的勝利
8.每間房間如果出入口都接上，會有bonus動作

RATING: 5
Get this thing away from me! After being such a big fan of Suburbia, Paulo and I were so excited to play the latest from Ted Alspach. And my oh my, did this game disappoint. First off, the box is 50% bigger than what it needs to be. Gorgeous artwork though it is, the components scarcely take up half of the inside. So poor design there. Secondly, it feels like half a game; one strong central mechanic (the master builder controlling the market) surrounded by a lot of meaningless and artless cardboard. Thirdly, most importantly, the game is simply not fun or intriguing in any way. I understand the appeal of Suburbia in the Middle Ages with just ONE CASTLE but the strategy is half of what it was in Suburbia; there's no give/take with points, the bonuses are incremental, and the cardboard bits are so underwhelming. For shame Alspach! I'll be around for your next game though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It's a tricky game to play well the first time, which is always a positive in a Euro, because it's hard to wrap your head around all the factors that go into the correct valuation of a room - the doors, the VPs, the bonus points, the closing bonus, its contribution to favor bonuses, and its usefulness/buildability to each player. Which means there's stuff to explore, but it also means that the first game doesn't allow the game to shine so well. There's little cheer/groan factor, instead the overriding emotions as the master builder who sets the valuations are one of damn, I undervalued / overvalued the buildings, or as one of the pickers one of quiet satisfaction at getting something you want cheaply, or having to make a decision on just how much you're willing to pay for the building you'd prefer. The other downside is that the game feels a bit repetitive - value buildings, take buildings, take buildings, take buildings, value buildings, take, take, take, etc. I like how the bonus cards lead players into different valuations and along different paths though. I'd play again to see if it ratchets up in enjoyment with more experience.

RATING: 4
I just do not get the hype with this one.  All the different shapes you can build with are supposed to be wacky fun.  I find the shapes in this game, for some reason, to be annoying.  I really dislike how infinitesimally small the icons on many of the tiles are.  This makes being the Master Builder more annoying than it should.  It is very difficult to strategically or tactically set prices as the Master Builder.  For one, values are very dependent on secret goal cards which are obviously hidden.  But much of the information that is supposed to be public is nearly impossible to see.  I am somewhat certain that if I just randomly arranged the tiles as Master Builder, my score would not really be worse.  The tiny money and tiny tiles and tiny icons all made this game a fiddly and annoying experience.  I would MUCH MUCH rather player Isle of Skye.

And one more complaint:  I HATE ZIGZAGGING SCORE TRACKS!

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Castles of Mad King Ludwig feels a lot like Alspach's earlier game, Suburbia--each turn, you buy a tile to add to your castle, trying to get it to align to both public and personal goals.  In this game, however, you're not trying to build up your income; instead, the game features a (mostly) closed economy--the "master builder" player sets the prices for all of the tiles, and players pay that player for the piece that they choose.  The castle tiles come in a variety of shapes and sizes, makes the game visually interesting as well as provides tile placement challenges.  These rooms all have a type, which determine what kind of bonus a player earns when the room is "completed" (all of the doors lead somewhere).  Some of these bonuses include another personal goal card, taking an extra turn, money, or points.  I really enjoyed this game--it was fun trying to figure out what tiles were worth to other players, and trying to build up my own castle.  The puzzle of putting my castle together to fulfill goals, complete rooms that were important to complete, and maintain flexibility for future turns was really engaging to me.

(5+ plays)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
As a big fan of Suburbia, I was really looking forward to this. It basically re-implements the earlier game with some exceptions. First, instead of the sliding market adding additional costs to tiles, each turn one player takes on the role of master builder. She arranges the market, setting a price for each room. Players then buy rooms, paying the master builder. Finally, the master builder buys and pays her money to the bank. This adds an interesting interaction wherein the master builder has to try to value each tile for each player, do some quick cost-benefit analyses, and set the market. This can lead to some AP issues. It wasn’t really an issue in my game because we were still learning and mostly took the attitude “we don’t care, let’s see what happens.” This part is also the only interaction in the game. The tiles players buy no longer interact with other players at all. This is something I enjoy in Suburbia, and I missed it here. However, there are more ways to make combos that score points in one’s own castle. Typically, just building a certain type of room can score additional points, and completely finishing a room (i.e., connecting all its doorways) grants additional bonuses. One aspect I appreciate more here than in Suburbia is the spatial element of laying out rooms. Because of the varied shape and sizes, the decision on where to place a room is much more interesting in Castles. 

After just one play, I think I prefer Suburbia, but either way I’m pretty certain I don’t need both.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

$0 - Birthday gift 2015


Goal Met :star::star::star::star::star: $1 or less per play

This game is a bit crunchier than I thought from looking at videos and reading reviews. There are multiple things you need to consider and it really is a bit brain burning so it's definitely a medium weighted game.

The theme is stronger than a lot of Euros out there so it's a plus in my fiancee's book.

RATING: 8
As someone who loves Suburbia, I had high expectations for this one and they were more or less met. I like puzzling out the best layout for optimal scoring, but in the end, building a castle is just fun. The price setting "master builder" mechanism is intriguing and can be quite agonizing. It's easy for a new or inexperienced player to set prices that can possibly inadvertently king-make, which is not ideal. With that said, CoMKL is still an enjoyable game from time to time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
In this game you draw an assortment of tiles, the active player decides the prices, then each player gets to buy before he does, paying him for the tiles. You add the tiles to the castle and score points for the tile and the effects of neighboring tiles should they matter. Then there are secret goals and public goals to get more points. It's like an alternate universe suburbia, which is more thematic, because the tiles are silly and in interesting shapes, and more interactive because you need to pay attention to what other players want and what they can afford to pay.

:star: Art, Iconography and components functional
:star: Clever or interesting mechanisms
:star: Reasonably low downtime between turns with mildly AP players
:star: Multiple plays stay interesting
:star: Low or mitigatable luck elements
:star::star::star::star::nostar: Raw enjoyment

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
A promising-looking "I cut, you choose" market mechanism where you set prices for others to extort money from them, and it disappears into this horrible miasma of set collection and awkward, arbitrary bonus assignment. 

In order to set prices effectively, one would have to know supply and demand. That's…kind of what effective market pricing is. And in this game, you remove room replenishment cards from the game before it begins, the result being that some room tiles might not be represented equally. That would be useful to know from a "supply" standpoint, no? What the supply actually is? (Spoiler: yes, yes it would be useful). And what do you do if you're dealt a bonus tile that rewards you for buying tiles that are going to be disproportionately held out of this particular game? And because of this uncertainty around Supply, you are unable to prioritize your own bonuses, not if it will take all game for you to discover that your pink room bonus won't amount to anything because too many pink room cards were shuffled out of the game back in the setup phase. 

Now on to "Demand." The first couple times around the table, you simply do not have enough information to know what other people's plans are, and so pricing is a little awkward. It is entirely possible that you price something low that is perfect for someone else. It is possible that the next player does this as well. And the main point here is that there are going to be some early turns where you're fishing for information rather than setting the correct balance between "Extortion" and "Temptation." Given a 15 turn game (roughly), you kind of have to waste three or so at the start because you simply don't know what people are targeting. And the bonuses can be pretty much anything, including "get one of each room type" (good luck spotting the player pursuing that one by round 5 or 6). From a risk perspective, you pretty much always have to assume that your private bonuses are underrepresented, and that turns the game into nothing more than maximizing tactical opportunity. In other words, the strategic stuff in the game is a distraction more than anything. 

I'm getting weary of Eurogames that are little more than gussied up Set Collection, and that's precisely what this one is. That it's in the Top 50 is crazy to me. This game's failure might be on me, though: I saw the market mechanism and thought "extortion and market manipulation in a multiplayer game!" and that is just [i]not[/i] what this game is at all. Yeah, yeah, I'm sure there are those who would say "not if you play with my group!" when I say it's not manipulative or aggressive enough, but holy crap, there are dozens of games that [i]are[/i] aggressive and manipulative that play faster, play better, and play cleaner than this. If my group is ever in the mood for market manipulation and extortion in 60-90 minutes, there's no way we'd ever choose this. This is a Eurogamer's definition of market manipulation; it is [i]not[/i] an economic gamer's definition of market manipulation. Player beware. 

The language of the game makes it look like an economic game, but it really isn't. It relies too much on the deliberate and uncommunicated withholding of information (both in the personal bonuses and in the removed replenishment cards). Not every game needs to be perfect information, but my goodness, exactly how does a player really take advantage of the chief innovation of this game if the information needed to do so is doled out in incomplete, deliberately unstable portions? In short, this is a mechanism in search of a game. And frankly, that game might be Coloretto.

RATING: 6
Pros:
1. Different. This is a tile laying game where the tiles aren't just squares with water, mountains, grass, villages etc. It's different rooms of a castle, with each room being graphically interesting.
2. Pricing system. I enjoy that the tiles are randomly drawn, but a player gets to rank and price them. This leads to a fun meta game.
3. Bonuses. It's not a game of just building high point value rooms. Considering connections bonuses and room completion keeps the game interesting.
4. App. This game has an app that is wonderfully done.

Cons:
1. Set up. This game is a pain to set up with each tile shape having to be separated out and stacked. I find it frustrating for a game that is a fairly quick play.
2. Luck of the draw. There's many tile laying games that suffer from this and this game doesn't do anything to address it. There are certain tiles that are very powerful and provide end game scoring. If those tiles always come out with bad timing, then you will likely lose. 
3. Table Footprint. The foot print for this game is extremely large. You will be building out your castle and it will likely not be tight and small. In fact, you're better off keeping it as spread out as possible to leave you many options. This means you need a very large table to play this game.

Summary: This is a very fun tile laying game. I would have zero problem ever playing this game given we have a play area large enough to handle it. However, the app version of this game is so well done and so much fun, that I don't have a reason to really ever buy the real version of this game. My recommendation would be to just spend the $6 for the app and let your friends buy the physical copy.

RATING: 7
This is a very interesting game. I have played a few games where you build out the playing area (ie, Suburbia, Betrayal). But this is the first one that I've seen that has such a great natural aesthetic feel. The pieces are all completely different sizes and you'll never build the same castle twice.

It does help that the rules are relatively simple. If a piece can logically fit, then you can place it! Of course, rooms have the NIMBY (not in my back yard) positioning restrictions that you have in City builders, which complicates things. Also, you'll be aiming for a different type of castle from your opponent, as you'll both have different hidden goals to strive for.

I've only played this 2 player - it's another game I think would get better with 3 or 4 players. But from what I've seen, I think it's quite a fun and interesting game that I think I would like to play a lot more often!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
29-03-2015. Rated after 1 play. Castles of Mad King Ludwig (Castles) is the follow-up game to Suburbia. In Castles, you try to build a strange castle, and get the most VPs for it. 

Each player starts the game with a foyer, a tile with 3 exits. Turns start by the master builder, setting prices for tiles by arranging the tiles. In turn order, all players can buy a tile starting with the player to the left of the master builder. If you buy the a tile, you must fit it into your castle. The tiles you buy give you points and restrict where they can be built by giving bonuses for building adjacent to some types of tiles (ie. gardens go great besides gardens) and penalties (music rooms next to bed rooms are stupid). If you finish a room, ie. all exits on the tile are alligned with other exits, you get a special bonus action, ranging from extra turns, more resources etc.You aim to get special end-game bonussen based on open information and some hidden information.

I didn't dislike this game. I think it's good, but I feel that Suburbia is a little clearer. I do like the graphical look of Castles better than suburbia, but the game is slower in comparison due to the master builder mechanisms. The master builder mechanisms is interesting, but also makes mistakes from the builder potentially game changing.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
--Feb2016-- It's an interesting set of constraints around a surprisingly random auction game.  One has a stream of interestingly valuable items (that is, the items are definitely not of the same value to each player) and yet the players have (rotating) responsibility to price those items.  So it's another take on the "I cut, you choose" thing: easy to get wrong; hard to get right.  It's far easier to throw the game to the left neighbour than make decisions that benefit oneself.
As a result, the game is plausibly better when all players are of the same familiarity and skill level with the game.  But even then, I'd worry that the room-arrival-randomness would become the dominant signal in the game.  So not likely something I'm demanding to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

It's a shame, because there is a lot of nice stuff in the rest of the game, but that ruins it for me.  I'll stick to Suburbia.

RATING: 9
Mindenképpen nagy negatívum volt a doboz kibontását követően, hogy egy relatíve óriási (=feleslegesen magas) dobozba képesek voltak insert nélkül belepakolni a kinyomó kartonokat. Ezt tetézi, hogy a viszonylag borsos árú játékból ugyanakkor képesek voltak kispórolni a műanyag tasakokat. Érthetetlen. A lapkák is lehetnének egy leheletnyivel vastagabbak, de főleg az igaz, hogy a kinyomó keretet hagyhatnák el könnyedebben...
A játék grafikáját sokan szidják, de szerintem irtózatosan hangulatos, és ami ennél is fontosabb, letisztult, egyértelmű és átlátható. Mindemellett az összes lapka egyedi.
A solo mód majdhogynem kissé megerőszakolása a játéknak. Közel sem annyira élvezetes, mint gondoltam. Gyakorlásnak viszont jó.

A játék veleje a különböző szobák árszintjének belövése, ami akár eredményezhet AP-s helyzetet, én mégsem éreztem ennek reális veszélyét. A játék nehézsége és játékideje nagyon jól van belőve. Szívesen játszom vele, nagyon hangulatos, hogy itt aztán tényleg nincs két egyforma parti. Ráadásul a végeredmény gyanánt elkészített kastély mindig büszkeséggel tölti el az ember szívét.
Nem egy full gémer, szerencsementes társas ez. Pont ezért nagyon szerethető. Kiemelkedő még benne a tetszés szerinti kombózhatóság is + a szabályok is logikusak és egyszerűek, egyáltalán nincs túlbonyolítva.
