RATING: 8
Visual Appeal: (3)
The game puts function above artistry. There are some nice details to the rooms that make them unique, but any beauty comes from the arrangement of the pieces during gameplay and not the pieces themselves.

Components: (3)
The cardboard is just thick enough to keep its shape. No storage insert, just bags which is fine with all the oddly shaped pieces. Cards have a nice flex and decent finish, but they are minis. Player aids are essential to remember bonus actions, and thankfully they are included!

Theme: (5)
From the rule book, "Players are contractors looking for the best deals on rooms while selling their services to other players." This is very evident through the adjustable auction track which can be used to price gouge your opponents for decent rooms. The story of the REAL King Ludwig II is in the rule book, so it's educational as well. The rooms are designed to make you twist and turn whimsically to make the most absurd combinations. Who wouldn't want a buttery attached to their bedroom? Or how about a bottomless pit at the bottom of a flight of stairs? What do you mean you put the Queen's bedroom behind the bowling alley? She'll lose sleep for sure with all that racket!

Learning: (4)
I find this game incredibly easy to teach. Check the goals, price the rooms, pay for rooms, build them and move on.

Gameplay: (4) 
 Figuring out how to plan rooms is tough, and the auction seems balanced enough with rooms accruing a discount over time. The one detractor is scoring. What a pain! You absolutely must score rooms immediately and correctly, because going back at the end to check the score is a chore. If you have to do it, make sure your remember the point handicap for the 2nd-4th players from the beginning of the game!

Overall Fun: (5)
I've never had a bad time playing this game with any number of players. It comes out very often because of how likeable and accessible it is, and it produces plenty of laughs and difficult choices.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
1-4p
Sleeves
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Alspach was a name we've never heard of until one year ago, when, by chance or coincidence, we watched a GameNight episode focused on a game called Ultimate Werewolf. The game sounded like a lot of fun, but, not having a big gaming group, we just kept an eye on the designer waiting for something different. So we got Suburbia, and was love at first sight. Rich, dense, deep, with countless interactions among tiles and one component that often is missing in many games: the freedom to go wherever you want, to develop your city in the way you thought being more interesting / rewarding. Could Castles of Mad King Ludwig be any better? Or at least at the same level?

The rules (honestly, badly structured and badly worded as all the Bezer Games rulebook we read so far) are quite simple. Rooms that will be used to build the castle are divided into ten stacks, according to their size (from 100 to 600 sqm). Inside each stack, tiles are randomized and then some of these (the exact number depends on the number of players) are returned to the box. In addition to the room stacks, there are two smaller stacks containing hallways and stairs. At this point, two separate deck of cards are prepared. The first one represent the "bonus cards": basically "secret objectives" that each player (and only him!) can score to gain extra points at the end of the game. The second deck (built on 11 cards / player) works as randomizer for new tiles to enter play. At this point, every player gains 15K Marks and the public objectives ("King's Favors") are revealed. All players can attempt achieving these.

The game begins with five cards from the randomizer being revealed (6 cards in a 3p game, 7 in a 4p game); on the board, there are a certain amount of spaces showing off a price (in a 2p game, ranging from 4K to 15K Marks) and the starting player ("Master Builder") decides what tile goes with one space. Id est, after tiles are placed on the board, there will be exactly one costing 4K, exactly one costing 6K, but which tile costs which price is decided by the Master Builder, who is the last one playing the round: the first player entitled to purchase a tile is the player on the left of the Master Builder, who pays the Master Builder (and not the bank) the cost of the tile. Once all other players have played, it's the Master Builder's turn to buy a tile, and he pays to the bank. Leftover tiles receive a cash bonus. Then a new round begins, with cards being drawn to replenish the reserve of tiles available for purchase. The new Master Builder may arrange the new tiles and the old tiles, according to his needs. As soon as all the cards in the randomizer are drawn, the game ends and you move to the final scoring. Points are also awarded during the game: each time a new tile is places, and when a room is completed (all doors lead to new rooms) special bonuses to resolve (depending on the type of room completed) are triggered.

The game plays fast, and it's incredible. It plays wonderfully 2p, and I suspect it will play wonderfully also 3p and 4p (should give the 3p option a try soonish). The auction mechanism is no proper auction mechanism: the price is set at the beginning of each turn, and it's set considering what tiles you want the other players to purchase and not to purchase, while you attempt to pursue your secret objectives and the common ones. So no obnoxious interactions where the guy with the most money dominates the game, and no absence of competition when played only 2p.

As said, the game is fast, 20-30 minutes and a 2p game is done; it plays faster than Suburbia and so far we are preferring it over Suburbia. Suburbia had a single strategy working better than others (maximize your income in the beginning of the game since the integral money earned over the game will be greater; and maximize your reputation later in the game, otherwise you'll be losing too much power during the game by repeatedly crossing the red barriers along the scoreboard), and some tiles that could lead to disastrous consequences (Casino, for instance, or the similar to the Casino tile that worked on reputation never decreasing), while this game appears (I haven't done the math for it yet) to be opened to many and different paths to victory.

As a plusvalore, some tiles are filled with little nerdy curiosities, such as a Suburbia or an Ultimate Werewolf image hidden as Easter egg on this or that tile.

Fantastic game.

RATING: 7
Very decent game of building a castle and scoring points along the way. Add a touch of open competitive scoring, some final game hidden scoring and you have what would typically be a points salad game... except it's a bit more interesting than that. Additionally there's a short decision process by the Master Builder each round where the six available buildings are put up for valuation for purchase. The money goes to the Master Builder's purse, so if she values too low or too high (without regard for what the opponents are looking for), then she'll miss out on potential income. Finally there's a spacial element as you add rooms to your castle and look for bonuses by closing off rooms and building the right type of adjacent rooms. I find the game to be just the right weight and it plays in about an hour. Rules are easy to explain and follow. The theme matches the mechanics enough to get my stamp of approval I found Castles of Mad King Ludwig to be a much better game than Suburbia. Only the fiddly retrospective scoring of some rooms prevents me from enjoying it just that bit more.

RATING: 7
Played once with 3P. The comparisons between Castles of Mad King Ludwig and [thing=123260][/thing] are inevitable but for the most part, Castles offers a few slight, but welcome, improvements over Suburbia. For one, the art is cheerier and more vivid, enabling tiles to be made out from afar. The scoring process is smoother too, mostly because the scoring does not depend on your opponent's build, so there is no need to constantly check if you score anything when your opponent is adding a tile to her castle. 

The biggest difference would be the inclusion of the Master Builder phase, which is a great way to redirect the player interaction found in Suburbia and to mitigate the luck involved in the order of which tiles appear. I do recognise though that this phase can induce downtime since it's possible to calculate how much each tile is worth to each player, although the secret goals screw this up a little. Also, seating order would be a major factor in players' performances if there is a gap in players' skills. But I'm partial towards the cake-cutting mechanism in general, and I think it's a worthy mechanism to make up for the loss of Suburbia's scoring track speed bumps.

The downside is that both secret and public goals have been maintained, and I still find the combination a little too swingy for my tastes especially if you have goals which sync with each other. The components are also of questionable quality since the board in the copy I played was peeling after a few games.

Still, Castles of Mad King Ludwig has enough tweaks for the better to make it the superior game among the two, or at least in my opinion. It still is a fairly enjoyable experience even if considered on its own, as there aren't too many games with the cake-cutting mechanism out there.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
[2015-06-14]
Kastélyépítés egy kis San Marcós előkészítéssel. A soron levő játékos a véletlenszerűen kijelölt különböző formájú szobát beárazza ellenfelei számára, akik miután ezekből egyet vásároltak ő maga is egyet megvehet és bővítheti az épülő kastélyát. Az ellenfelek mindig az építőnek fizetnek, ami miatt jól meggondolandóan kell eltervezi az árakat, hogy keressünk is vele, de a magunknak szánt épületet azért lehetőség szerint ne vigyék el. Megvan az AP veszély rendesen, de érdekes módon nem éreztem vészesnek az első játék alkalmával. Másik AP veszélyes hely, a megszerzett kastélyépület leépítése az eddigi épületünkbe. Különböző formájú és alakú lapkák adnak egy fix pontot és még bónusz a szomszédjaikért. Emiatt is is érdemes rendesen előre tervezni. A játékban minden befejezett szoba ad még erős bónuszt. Van három nyílt győzelmi pont szerző cél, mint a Kingdom Builderben, de szerezhető titkosak is. Szóval tulajdonképpen sok újdonságot nem kapunk, de egy jól összerakott játékot igen. A játék szép, jól néznek ki az épített kastélyok és bőven van változatosság. Tetszett, de nem rohannék a boltba érte egy jól összeválogatott 100-as társaskészlet mellé új játéknak.

RATING: 7
1 play

Castles is a pretty fun game! Players are building their own private castles using tiles of various shapes. Each tile is a different type of room or hallway, with specific rules for placement. They come up in random order (card deck), and each round the current player sets the price for all of them; but all other players buy first. There's also a deck of bonus cards, awarding VP for a wide variety of conditions; players acquire these as they buy certain tiles. When the game ends, score points based on your builds and the bonus cards -- most VP wins!

I had a hard time figuring out how to price stuff (I tend to prefer more multiplayer solitaire-y stuff where I don't have to look at everyone else's board so much). 

Good game, better than Suburbia -- I would like to play it again!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
JH
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I am admittedly a fan of very heavy and meaty games.  The denser the rules and the more varied my options the better usually.  This is to a fault as I often find myself uninterested in lighter fare which makes a good chunk of my library not very appealing to a broad group of people.

With that introduction into my tastes though know that I very much so enjoyed this game which is very light and accessible, it just charmed my pants off.  The game is going to look and feel very similar to Suburbia, also from Ted Alspach, but this time players are building a castle instead of a city.

I also really like Suburbia but this game does a lot of things that I think make it even better.  First of all instead of tiles just sliding down the cost track when bought each round the "master builder", a role passed to the next player at the beginning of each round, gets to order the tiles on the prices.  Then all the other players get to buy tiles from this list, paying the master builder, before he gets to purchase.  I love this simple mechanic that presents such deep decisions for the master builder.  Obviously he wants to make the tiles he plans to buy the cheapest but he also has to worry about other players snatching it out from under him before he gets a chance.

The varied room sizes and shapes is also another great change.  You have to take care not only which tiles you put next to which, much like in Suburbia, but you actually have to ensure the tiles you would like to place can even fit and plan ahead where you might like to put future tiles.  Not to mention it is just really intriguing to see the wacky shapes and layouts of each person's castle.

"The bathroom?  Sure, just step outside, walk through the shed and the piano room, go downstairs, through the dungeon, and then back up the stairs you will find it just through the dressing room."
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Players take it in turn to be the "Master Builder", offering various different types of rooms for a variety of prices. The other players can either pass, earning $5000, or pay the asking price and place the room in their castle.

There are various restrictions on adjacency, entrances etc., and different kinds of rooms do different things when they are "completed" (i.e., all of their exits connect to something), such as give points, money, bonus cards (worth points at the end of the game) or allow placement of another room.

It's a light tile placement game with its limited interaction provided care of the price-setting mechanism (Baby's First Container?). Much of the charm is in the rooms themselves, particularly the bizarre basements like the Venus Grotto and Secret Lair; that the tiles aren't abstract hexagons is a significant improvement over Alspach's city-building games, like Suburbia.

RATING: 7.2
Ted Alspach spends a lot of time on development of his games, and you can tell from this design. It is very, very streamlined, with very simple and short rules.
The game has quite some [thing=123260][/thing] in it. It also has tiles which you can buy for a varying price, the tile you buy gives you points and additional points according to what other tiles are adjacent or elsewhere in your castle. Very similar indeed. What is quite different is the I-split-you-choose mechanism which we know from games like [thing=1041][/thing]. Except here you don't make sets, you determine prices. You want to make things expensive so the other players pay you lots of money. But if you make it too expensive, they will buy something cheaper. And you want to make the tiles you want cheap, but not so cheap the other players will snatch them from under your nose.
It all works great together and it plays very nicely and quick. I'm not sure if this is as good as Suburbia, but it certainly is a very solid design.

Initial rating: 7.2/10
BGG scale: 8/10
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
I'm guilty of many, many rants against Bezier Games, having disliked Ultimate Werewolf, One Night Ultimate Werewolk, Suburbia and even managing to dislike Start Player, so it was with some trepidation that I sat down to play Castles...

We started setting up and I saw many of the same components from Suburbia - that terrible price track that makes the game so random with more than two players, stacks of tiles that didn't look like they were all going to be in the game - all of the issues I'd had with Suburbia were flooding back to me and my heart sank.

And I'm delighted to say that they appear to have been addressed; now, tiles are repositioned on the price track by players meaning no more lucky draws determining games, there is some control over the tiles that come into play, but most imortantly you can't just simply lose because the type of tile you decided to go heavily into just isn't in the game (airports were famously bad for this in Suburbia). All in all, Castles of Mad King Ludwig delivered a really smooth, fun play experience. To the extent that it has now become the first Bezier Game in my collection - and that is quite something coming from me.

RATING: 10
This has become a staple game of my gaming collection and for good reason. You are putting together a blueprint for a castle filled with long hallways, music rooms, bedrooms, outdoor yards, dungeon rooms, and more in a design that you hope will please the Mad King. Players take turns as the master builder and assigns costs to the available rooms to build, then the other players buy and design their castles to score the maximum amount of points. 

What results are some wildly designed castles that are fascinating to look at. You begin looking at what other players are trying to accomplish and try to price desirable pieces high enough to get paid as the master builder. It's a blast and I wholeheartedly recommend this game.

RATING: 8
Enjoying this one very much. Interestingly, my wife likes it, as well, but for very different reasons. She like building the castle and looking for ways to place the rooms so that bonuses work together well. I enjoy the other half of the game more - the auctions - which involves the setting of prices and acquisition of rooms. The only real downsides to this clever little game are that the castles can sprawl right off the table if you don't have a very large play space, and the whole thing can break down if you have inexperienced or clueless players who are unable to value the rooms correctly enough to set prices well (although is is something of a danger with most auction games, here it is very pronounced with the person to the left of the poor auctioneer reaping all the benefits). In any even, there's quite a bit to like here and Castles of Mad King Ludwig seems destined to hit my game table quite a bit in the coming days.

RATING: 8
An excellent castle building game, that is simple to learn but very interactive.

I love games that allow you to play against the other players, keeping things really simple and giving it all to the interaction. Castles of Mad King Ludwing is exactly such a game. The rules are deceptively simple, and set up the stage for an excellent drafting mechanism. As an added bonus, you are building a castle, and it's going to be completely different from game to game, with every room being unique. The game finally flows well and fast and feels really rewarding in the end even if you end up last.

The bad? Well, the components could have been better - say bigger and better illustrated. But that would end up taking more space on the table which is a different problem. Some downtime and a lot of frustration might be a bigger issue, especially with new players when they take on the master builder role, as it's often difficult to price the rooms. It's also very easy for master builders to make mistakes that swing the game heavily in one player's favor. Aka it's chaotic for the first games of any player, for all players. This is not a new player friendly game like suburbia.

Overall it's an very good game, but though pretty much everyone can play it and enjoy it, it really does need an experienced group to shine.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
I had only a vague idea that this was some sort of tile laying game along the lines of Suburbia and wasn't sure quite what to expect. As the game was put out on the table, I was a little taken back by the number of bits and how much information you needed to absorb for each one. Trying to read the tiles was a bit of a challenge. Once you play the game a few times I'm sure you'll start to remember tiles. For the first play, I decided to ignore other player's tiles for the most part.

The primary challenge in the game is playing the master builder and valuing all the buildings that come out for the turn. You want to price buildings so that people buy them and give you money. You won't likely get any more money until your next master builder turn, so this is pretty important. You want to make the things they want to buy expensive enough that they pay you a good chunk of change, but not pricey enough that they decide not to buy.

Oh, but if the tile is too good and will score them a ton of points, you want to price it out of their range. And since you will be buying last in the turn, you want to figure out a way to price the building you really want such that it won't get bought by the other players, but will still be available to you at a reasonable price.

The rest of the game is deciding which buildings to buy, which keys into all sorts of factors. First and foremost is how much money you have left and how long it will be until you get more. But rooms score points for being built, some rooms score points for being connected to other types of rooms, and some rooms just score points for having other types of rooms in your castle. So figuring out the room that gives you the most points is a big consideration.

But then there's all sorts of placement restrictions, the first of which is simply geometry... will your room fit where you want to put it? And some rooms don't like being next to other rooms, and some rooms can't have anything adjacent to them, and some rooms are basement rooms and can't adjoin the first floor rooms without stairs. And rooms give bonuses when you complete them (which requires matching every exit to an entrance of another room) depending of the type of room. Which can give you all sorts of powerful options. And players have different personal goals and a few common goals.

Despite the myriad of things to take into consideration when choosing which room to build, pricing the rooms as master builder is still the harder part of the game. Because to accurately value things you'd need to go through the same "What should I build?" calculation for every player. Trying to do this to any degree of precision would pretty much kill the game and send it into an AP-type time distortion field. I basically shortcut this step by picking a top level note for each player (that player wants sleeping rooms, that player wants basement rooms, that player wants dining rooms) and not worrying about it too much. Thinking about it much more than that would make the game less fun and more spreadsheety... so there is at least one player that I know I'd never play this game with (Sorry, Gwen).

I really enjoyed this game. There were a lot of interesting decisions and I found the game challenging and competitive. It certainly has a certain feel of Suburbia to it, but while Suburbia feels pretty dry and themeless, I found this game much more thematically engaging. It was fun looking at the castles all of the players built and seeing what sorts of madness was going on. The cooks in one player's castle would have to take a quick romp through a nine pin bowling lane in order to get supplies for dinner? Crazy!

I felt more invested in my castle more than I ever felt invested in any city in Suburbia. How the castle developed was much more dependent on my decisions than in Suburbia, primarily due to the odd shapes and placement restrictions. You just don't see that with the hexagonal pieces in Suburbia as it's hard to muck things up too much with pieces of only one shape.

And it's much easier to view the buildings in Suburbia as generic blue/yellow/etc. hexagonal pieces whereas the buildings in CMKL with their distinctive shapes and imagery help you retain the idea that they are specific rooms with different functions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[thing=155426][/thing] is an strategy game about tile placement, where players build their castles while paying the others for parts to use or receiving money when its their turn to set prices. 

The game revolves around managing money - as it can go really fast - while seeking the best opportunities, both to buy cheap and to take what you need to score better, as some rooms work better with others, and finishing a room (connecting all the openings) gives bonus depending on the type of room - therefore going for pieces that connect well (in a Tetris-like manner) is important also.

It is very good that the mechanisms are well done, because the game looks awful - never once I was proud of what I built, as the castles always looked bad, with loads of cornes, huge circles, corridors and others parts in odd ways - this feel was shared by the others; not the really beautiful structures we think when talking about castles - still, Ludwig is mad. Anyway, I usually enjoy games where something is built during play, yet in here I don't find this pleasure, since I know that it will likely look ugly in the end.

However, as I mentioned, the mechanics are solid and give enough to think about and to have fun with, that I don't mind the ugliness all that much. They provided several possibilities for choices and decisions, giving many paths to achieve victory. In the choice of style of substance the designer went for the proper sutff, yet i wish that [thing=155426][/thing] could have both.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
I am not sure how Castles of Mad King Ludwig is getting such high ratings. This game is purely a solitaire exercise with all players focused on building their own castles. Interaction is basically very low to non-existent. Sure, there is some jostling to pick up castle tiles, but there is very little you can do to alter other peoples selection. You just have to pick whatever is leftover when you turn comes along.
Still, the low interaction is not the biggest let down, The game is inherently fiddly. Lots of little points here and there to calculate with each move. I find that distracting and bothersome. It drains the game from momentum. The scoring categories are just too broad. For instance, every dungeon tile contributes to continuous scoring and every adjacent tile can also trigger scoring. Plus, re-scoring can occur and so can extra turns. Not to mention utility cards and king's favor scoring. All these scoring efforts makes a really thematic game cumbersome and unnecessarily convoluted. 

I really wish someone could take Castles and turn scoring around so that it has fewer scoring categories. Perhaps even one?

04/2017: Additional plays of the game with different player counts did not change my rating, but just affirmed all the shortcomings of the game. Scoring is fiddly and the spatial puzzle wasn't as immersive. It also dragged.

RATING: 6
A clear follow-on to [gameid=123260], this is virtually a reimplementation with a few tweaked mechanics and a different theme.  I prefer the first one, no doubt partially because I was introduced to it first, but it also fits better with the theme.  The idea of competing castles doesn't make much sense, nor does the idea that there's a limited number of plans that form a lot to draw from.  There's also some very artificial chaos thrown into the mix here, and if your opponents choose not to pay you for their own reasons, you can really get stuck behind the 8-ball.  The game contains a certain opportunistic playstyle which appeals to me, but it's ultimately not enough to save a very artificial-feeling pile of mechanics.

RATING: 7
It's hard not to compare this to Suburbia, since they share so much of the same DNA. Still, the comparisons are tough because almost every aspect of it has something I like better than Suburbia and something I liked better in Suburbia as well. I like the new market, because it adds so much game space but I dislike it because it slows the game down and can just flat out not work among players of differing experience. I love the free form laying of the tiles, instead of the strict hex grid, but a dislike that a lot of the puzzle aspects are lost. (It's there in a different way, but much easier and less satisfying to work around in Ludwig). I like the tile combo chains in Suburbia much better, as they are more intuitive, but the moves in Ludwig can be even bigger, which is also fun.

I guess what I'm saying is that there isn't a good reason to prefer one over the other. I think I like Suburbia better, but there are enough new experiences in Ludwig to make owning and playing it worth while. Especially when you consider the table talk. Thematically explaining why a player built the kitchen next to the stables is the best part of the game and adds to the fun exponentially.

Final word...Suburbia is a better game, but Castles of Mad King Ludwig will make you laugh a whole lot more. Choose your fun accordingly!

RATING: 5
Suburbia v2
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
For the longest time, I wrote off Castles of Mad King Ludwig as an also-ran of Suburbia. It looked more fiddly with the cards and multiple tiles, and I knew my anal retentiveness would flare up with the various little rooms being disturbed from their perfect right angles. However, I’ve been doing a lot of trading lately and decided to get rid of a game I realized I wasn’t going to play and traded for this one and one other.

When it showed up, I did my normal “new” game routine of breaking everything out and organizing it as best I can given the tackle boxes I own and the plastic baggies I have on hand. After that, I brought it downstairs and it sat for a few days until my toddler was cooperative enough for me to try a solo game of it.

I was blown away. Castles is not an also-ran of Suburbia, though it’s clearly an iteration of it. The real differences come in the form of only worrying about points (and not income, reputation, and population) and paying money to the other players (when that player is the first player) instead of the bank.

Additionally, at the end of Suburbia you would have quite the city…of hexagons. It was impressive to look at from a mechanical level, but it was pretty bland looking. With Castles you have this unique castle built to your specifications with odd bits and bobs here and there. I mean, who knew you needed four gardens and no bedrooms?

Money is very interesting in Castles. In Suburbia, you always paid the bank when you bought a tile, but in Castles you only pay the bank when you’re the master builder (first player). The other players end up paying the master builder the cost of the room and it’s up to the master builder to correctly price the buildings in order to get the most money from the other players. This is done in a I-cut-you-choose mechanism where the master builder places the buildings and the other players buy and when everyone else has taken their turn, the master builder gets to buy something. I absolutely love this mechanic.

The last trace of Suburbia in Castles would be the King’s Favors. Instead of having goals where only the person who had the most gets points, now everyone gets some amount of points, just decreasing with your rank. In addition, the secret goals in Suburbia have been replaced with bonus cards which only affect you and not the other players and are typically more of a bonus for each of something or for having a certain condition, not for having the most or least of something.

All of that aside, I think Castles has some disadvantages to it. First, there’s a good deal of shuffling at the beginning, though since you’re shuffling fewer tiles, I think it’s better than Suburbia. Second, the castle rooms move around and you find yourself reorganizing more often then you like and it takes a bit more time than it did in Suburbia.

However, I just spent several paragraphs telling you that I really liked Castles and only one telling you about it’s disadvantages. I quite like this game and I’m happy I finally picked it up because this is one that’ll be in my collection for a while.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Mini Chimera (Red) 1
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
We are master builders commissioned by King Ludwig to satisfy his obsession of building the most extravagant castle ever. The meat of the game is in the auction phase. In turn order the master builders draw random blueprints to determine and arrange the price of available castle room tiles of various shapes and sizes. Unselected tiles are incentivized with a gold token. Manage your gold to build your castle with tile placement trying to achieve various tile collection objectives for points. Tie breaker goes to the largest square footage castle. Give yourself some table space for this.

RATING: 9.5
The Castles of Mad King Ludwig is still in that honeymoon period of a game I really like. This rating is likely to fall a bit over time but presently I really enjoy this game.

The mechanics inform the theme and inform the mechanics. "Mad Castles" indeed. Everyone at the table constantly laughs about the stories their castles entail. The randomness is minimal which keeps the strategy/tactics on point during the game. The random elements give you extra sub goals of which there are plenty of.

The pieces are easy to organize, rules easy to learn, and the production quality is high.

I don't like to use cost as part of a rating but Ludwig is also reasonably cheap for its package which is fantastic.

I haven't played suburbia which I know is similar. But in a vacuum, Ludwig is an absolute must buy.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I saw a documentary on the real (and not really mad) King Ludwig just before I first heard about this game, and immediately knew I wanted it. I think it's a bit more expensive than it needed to be for a box with so little in it, but on the whole, I like it a lot.

There's the fun of building an extremely eccentric castle, and the satisfaction of getting nice synergy between rooms, and the brain-meltingness of trying to decide how to price the rooms when you're Master Builder.

But it has some serious production issues, in particular the pathetic flimsy multi-piece boards for putting the cards and tiles on and keeing the score, and the annoyingly hard-to-read zigzag score track. The art is pretty feeble too, although the rooms tiles themselves are quite nice.

It's also longer than I'd consider ideal, and removing tiles for 3 and especially 2 players is annoyingly fiddly, and can also harm your ability to achieve some bonuses (you can just use the setup for 4 players, but it will take even longer).

RATING: 8
I greatly enjoy the sense of building something in this game and in other games.  Many have commented on not enjoying the master builder aspect of this game but I find it to be a necessary mechanic in the game and one that adds tension and enjoyment to other players.  The game is great at all player counts and is very thematic with is a nice change from many other euros.  As an added bonus, my wife loves the game so this is a definite keeper.  One downside to the game, is the components are very cheaply produced.  The cardboard is thin, but it is sturdy.  The game has iconography but it is very easy to understand.  Castles of Mad King Ludwig is one of our favorite games and as I said before, I love how when you're done with the game, even if you lose, you feel like you have built something cool and unique.

Note:  Sleeved with Premium Mayday Mini Chimera (red pack)

Note: Meeple Realty Insert

RATING: 7

Aside from that it's a delightfully meaty game.  The pricing-of-rooms mechanic is excellent, though it can lead to some AP.  Experience is a HUGE bonus in this game.

RATING: 10
This game is great. The building/buying mechanic makes for tons of fun and also incorporates tons of strategy. The fact that there are so many different ways to score points (between the common goals, each player's secret goals, and the different bonuses for each completed room) keeps the game fresh because you'll always find yourself coming up with a different strategy each game since some rooms that seemed completely worthless in the previous game suddenly become the most sought after and pricey in the next, and vice versa. Plus, it's just fun to build your own castle!
